Thirteen propositions: among them simple—which are those whose reality is only affirmation or only negation—and among them compound—which are those whose reality is composed of affirmation and negation together. As for the simple ones, they are six. The first: absolute necessary, which is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate's affirmation to the subject or its negation from it as long as the subject's essence exists, like our saying: 'Necessarily every human is an animal' and 'Necessarily nothing of human is a stone.' The second: absolute perpetual, which is that in which judgment is made of the perpetuity of the predicate's affirmation to the subject or its negation from it as long as the subject's essence exists; its example in affirmation and negation is what passed. The third: general conditional, which is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate's affirmation to the subject or its negation from it under the condition of the subject's description, like our saying: 'Necessarily every writer is moving fingers as long as he is a writer' and 'Necessarily nothing of the writer is still-fingered as long as he is a writer.' The fourth: general customary, which is that in which judgment is made of the perpetuity of the predicate's affirmation to the subject or its negation from it under the condition of the subject's description; its example in affirmation and negation is what passed. The fifth: absolute general, which is that in which judgment is made of the predicate's affirmation to the subject or its negation from it actually, like our saying: 'By absolute generality, every human is breathing' and 'By absolute generality, nothing of human is not-breathing.' The sixth: general possible, which is that in which judgment is made of the removal of absolute necessity from the side contrary to the judgment, like our saying: 'By general possibility, every fire is hot' and 'By general possibility, nothing of fire is cold.'
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage defines and enumerates six types of simple modal propositions. The author begins by stating that there are thirteen kinds of propositions (elsewhere enumerated) among which are simple and compound. Simple propositions are those whose meaning consists of only affirmation or only negation. Then he lists the six simple modal propositions: (1) absolute necessary (daruriyyah mutlaqah): judgment of necessity as long as the subject's essence exists, e.g., 'Necessarily every human is an animal' (affirmation) and 'Necessarily no human is a stone' (negation). (2) absolute perpetual (da'imah mutlaqah): judgment of perpetuity (continuous truth) as long as the subject's essence exists; example is the same as the first. (3) general conditional (mashrutah 'ammah): judgment of necessity conditioned on the subject's description, e.g., 'Necessarily every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer' and the negation. (4) general customary ('urfiyyah 'ammah): judgment of perpetuity conditioned on the subject's description; example as before. (5) absolute general (mutlaqah 'ammah): judgment of actual truth without any temporal condition, e.g., 'By absolute generality, every human is breathing' (which is true actually but not necessarily). (6) general possible (mumkinah 'ammah): judgment that removes absolute necessity from the opposite side, i.e., the judgment is possibly true, e.g., 'By general possibility, every fire is hot' (not necessary, but possible). The examples are drawn from everyday and scientific contexts, following the Islamic tradition's use of concrete illustrations.

Simple and Compound Propositions in Modal Logic
Author defines simple and compound propositions and clarifies the status of modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish simple from compound propositions and identify which modal propositions are truly compound.

Core Idea
Not all modal propositions are compound; only those that combine affirmation and negation are.

This passage from Qutb al-Din al-Razi's logic commentary establishes a critical distinction between simple and compound propositions. The author uses examples from modal logic to illustrate the criterion and warns against the common error of treating all modal statements as compound. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly analyzing the logical structure of propositions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining criterion
The author states that a proposition is simple if it contains only one judgment (affirmation or negation), and compound if it contains both. Locate this criterion in the Arabic: 'القضية إما بسيطة أو مركبة؛ لأنها إن اشتملت على حكمين مختلفين بالإيجاب والسلب فهي مركبة، وإلا فبسيطة.'
2
Examine the simple proposition examples
Look at the examples given: 'كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة' (simple affirmative) and 'لاشيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة' (simple negative). Notice that the modality 'necessarily' does not add a second judgment; the proposition remains simple.
3
Examine the compound proposition example
Now consider the compound example: 'كل إنسان كاتب بالفعل لا دائماً'. This proposition affirms writing for humans and negates its perpetuity. The negation is expressed dependently (لا دائماً), which makes it one compound proposition.
4
Understand the role of dependent expression
The author emphasizes that if the negation were expressed independently (e.g., as a separate proposition), there would be two distinct propositions. The dependent phrase 'لا دائماً' integrates the negation into the same proposition, creating compoundness.
5
Apply the distinction to modal propositions
The key takeaway: 'فكل قضية مركبة تكون موجهة وليس كل موجهة مركبة' – every compound proposition is modal, but not every modal proposition is compound. Modalities like necessity and perpetuity do not introduce a second judgment, while negated modalities (e.g., 'not perpetually') do.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضية إما بسيطة أو مركبة؛ لأنها إن اشتملت على حكمين مختلفين بالإيجاب والسلب فهي مركبة، وإلا فبسيطة. فالقضية البسيطة هي التي حقيقتها - أي معناها - إما إيجاب فقط، كقولنا : «كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة» - فإن معناه ليس إلا إيجاب الحيوانية للإنسان - وإما سلب فقط ، كقولنا : «لاشيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة» - فإن حقيقته ليست إلا سلب الحجرية عن الإنسان. والقضية المركبة هي التي حقيقتها تكون ملتئمة من الإيجاب والسلب ، كقولنا : كلّ إنسان كاتب بالفعل لا دائماً» فإن معناه إيجاب الكتابة للإنسان وسلبها عنه بالفعل. (1) إذا حكمت بإيجاب المحمول للموضوع أوّلاً ثم حكمت بينهما بسلب لا بعبارة مستقلة - بل بعبارة غير مستقلة دالة على كيفية تلك النسبة الإيجابية - يعد المجموع قضية واحدة مركبة، كقولنا : «كلّ إنسان ضاحك لادائماً»، فإن قولنا «لا دائماً» يدلّ على أن تلك النسبة الإيجابية بينهما ليست بدائمة، فيكون السلب واقعاً بالفعل، وإلا لكان الإيجاب دائماً ، فمن حيث دلالته على كيفية النسبة يكون جهة للقضية، ومن حيث دلالته على الحكم السلبي يكون موجباً لتركب القضية. وإنما قلنا الا بعبارة مستقلة» لأنه إذا عبر عن الحكم السلبي بعبارة مستقلة كان هناك قضيتان مستقلتان - لاقضيّة واحدة مركبة - وكذا الحال إذا حكمت أولا بالسلب بينهما ثم حكمت بالإيجاب على تلك الطريقة. فكل قضية مركبة تكون موجهة وليس كل موجهة مركبة، فإن اعتبار الضرورة والدوام لا يوجب تركيب القضية، إذ لم يحصل بسببهما بين الموضوع والمحمول حكمان مختلفتان إيجاباً وسلباً، بخلاف اللاضرورة واللادوام، لأنهما يوجبان حكماً آخر مخالفاً للحكم السابق في الإيجاب والسلب كماسيأتي تحقيقه (شریف).
The proposition is either simple or compound; because if it includes two different judgments of affirmation and negation, it is compound, otherwise it is simple. So the simple proposition is that whose reality—i.e., its meaning—is either only affirmation, like our saying: 'Every human is an animal necessarily'—for its meaning is nothing but affirmation of animality for the human—or only negation, like our saying: 'Nothing of human is a stone necessarily'—for its reality is nothing but negation of stone-ness from the human. And the compound proposition is that whose reality is composed of affirmation and negation, like our saying: 'Every human is a writer actually, not perpetually'—for its meaning is affirmation of writing for the human and negation of it from him actually. (1) If you first judge affirmation of the predicate for the subject, then you judge between them a negation by a dependent expression—not by an independent expression—the whole is considered one compound proposition, like our saying: 'Every human is laughing, not perpetually', for our saying 'not perpetually' indicates that that affirmative relation between them is not perpetual, so the negation occurs actually, otherwise the affirmation would be perpetual. So from the aspect of its indication of the quality of the relation, it is a modality (jihah) of the proposition, and from the aspect of its indication of the negative judgment, it necessitates the compoundness of the proposition. And we said 'by a dependent expression' because if one expresses the negative judgment by an independent expression, there would be two independent propositions, not one compound proposition. And the same applies if you first judge negation between them, then judge affirmation in that manner. So every compound proposition is modal (muwajjahah), but not every modal proposition is compound; for considering necessity and perpetuity does not necessitate compoundness of the proposition, since by them there does not occur between subject and predicate two different judgments in affirmation and negation, unlike non-necessity and non-perpetuity, because they necessitate another judgment contrary to the previous judgment in affirmation and negation, as will be verified later. (Sharif).
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage defines simple and compound propositions and clarifies the relationship between modality and compoundness. The author states that a proposition is either simple or compound: simple if it contains only one judgment (affirmation or negation), compound if it contains both. For example, 'Every human is an animal necessarily' is simple because it only affirms animality; 'Nothing of human is a stone necessarily' is simple because it only negates stone-ness. A compound proposition combines affirmation and negation, e.g., 'Every human is a writer actually, not perpetually'—this affirms writing for humans but negates its perpetuity. The key point is that a compound proposition must involve two judgments (affirmation and negation) expressed in a single statement with a dependent phrase, like 'not perpetually' in 'Every human is laughing, not perpetually.' If the negation were expressed independently, we would have two separate propositions. The author then distinguishes between modality (jihah) and compoundness: a compound proposition always has a modality (because it includes a qualification), but not every modal proposition is compound. Modalities like necessity and perpetuity (al-darurah wa al-dawam) do not in themselves create compoundness because they do not introduce a second judgment contrary to the first. For instance, 'Every human is an animal necessarily' is modal but not compound. However, modalities like 'not necessarily' or 'not perpetually' (al-la-darurah wa al-la-dawam) do create compoundness because they imply a negation of the original modality, resulting in two judgments. The passage ends with a reference to a later investigation (by Sharif al-Jurjani).

Key Terms
القضية البسيطة
القضية المركبة
الموجهة
الجهة

B) كل إنسان كاتب بالفعل لا دائماً
C) بالإمكان العام كل نار حارة
D) بالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر

Key Takeaway
A compound proposition combines affirmation and negation in one dependent expression; not every modal proposition qualifies as compound—only those with a negated modality introduce a second judgment.

Simple Propositions: Pure Affirmation or Negation
Author defines the simple proposition and gives two examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a simple proposition and distinguish it from a compound one based on whether it contains only affirmation or only negation.

Core Idea
A simple proposition makes only one judgment: either purely 'yes' or purely 'no'.

In Islamic logic, propositions are the building blocks of arguments. But not all propositions are alike. The simplest kind is one that either affirms something of something or denies it, with no mixing. This lesson unpacks that definition using the author's example from the source.

Guided Path
1
Understand the criterion
A simple proposition's reality (ḥaqīqa) is either affirmation only or negation only. There is no combination within its meaning.
2
Examine the first example
Consider 'Every human is an animal by necessity' (كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة). It only affirms animality of humanity. There is no negation anywhere.
The phrase 'by necessity' is a modality but does not add a second judgment; it qualifies the single affirmative judgment.
3
Examine the second example
Consider 'Nothing of human is a stone by necessity' (لاشيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة). It only negates stoneness of humanity. No affirmation is present.
4
Test your understanding
For any proposition, ask: Does its meaning contain both affirmation and negation? If no, it is simple.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقضية البسيطة هي التي حقيقتها - أي معناها - إما إيجاب فقط، كقولنا : «كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة» - فإن معناه ليس إلا إيجاب الحيوانية للإنسان - وإما سلب فقط ، كقولنا : «لاشيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة» - فإن حقيقته ليست إلا سلب الحجرية عن الإنسان.
والقضية المركبة هي التي حقيقتها تكون ملتئمة من الإيجاب والسلب ، كقولنا : كلّ إنسان كاتب بالفعل لا دائماً» فإن معناه إيجاب الكتابة للإنسان وسلبها عنه بالفعل.
(1) إذا حكمت بإيجاب المحمول للموضوع أوّلاً ثم حكمت بينهما بسلب لا بعبارة مستقلة - بل بعبارة غير مستقلة دالة على كيفية تلك النسبة الإيجابية - يعد المجموع قضية واحدة مركبة، كقولنا : «كلّ إنسان ضاحك لادائماً»، فإن قولنا «لا دائماً» يدلّ على أن تلك النسبة الإيجابية بينهما ليست بدائمة، فيكون السلب واقعاً بالفعل، وإلا لكان الإيجاب دائماً ، فمن حيث دلالته على كيفية النسبة يكون جهة للقضية، ومن حيث دلالته على الحكم السلبي يكون موجباً لتركب القضية.
وإنما قلنا الا بعبارة مستقلة» لأنه إذا عبر عن الحكم السلبي بعبارة مستقلة كان هناك قضيتان مستقلتان - لاقضيّة واحدة مركبة - وكذا الحال إذا حكمت أولا بالسلب بينهما ثم حكمت بالإيجاب على تلك الطريقة. فكل قضية مركبة تكون موجهة وليس كل موجهة مركبة، فإن اعتبار الضرورة والدوام لا يوجب تركيب القضية، إذ لم يحصل بسببهما بين الموضوع والمحمول حكمان مختلفتان إيجاباً وسلباً، بخلاف اللاضرورة واللادوام، لأنهما يوجبان حكماً آخر مخالفاً للحكم السابق في الإيجاب والسلب كماسيأتي تحقيقه (شریف).
The simple proposition is that whose reality - i.e., its meaning - is either affirmation only, as our saying: 'Every human is an animal by necessity' - for its meaning is nothing but affirmation of animality of human - or negation only, as our saying: 'Nothing of human is a stone by necessity' - for its reality is nothing but negation of stoneness from human.
And the compound proposition is that whose reality is composed of affirmation and negation, as our saying: 'Every human is actually a writer, not always' - for its meaning is affirmation of writing for human and its negation from him actually.
(1) If you first judge the affirmation of the predicate to the subject, then judge between them a negation with a non-independent expression - rather a dependent expression indicating the modality of that affirmative relation - the whole is counted as one compound proposition, as our saying: 'Every human is laughing, not always'; for our saying 'not always' indicates that that affirmative relation between them is not permanent, so the negation actually occurs, otherwise the affirmation would be permanent. So from the perspective of its indication of the modality of the relation, it is a modality of the proposition; and from the perspective of its indication of the negative judgment, it necessitates the composition of the proposition.
And we only said 'with a non-independent expression' because if one expresses the negative judgment with an independent expression, there would be two independent propositions - not one compound proposition. And similarly if you first judge negation between them then judge affirmation in that manner. So every compound proposition is modal, but not every modal proposition is compound; for consideration of necessity and perpetuity does not necessitate composition of the proposition, since there are not due to them two different judgments of affirmation and negation between subject and predicate. Unlike non-necessity and non-perpetuity, because they necessitate another judgment contrary to the previous judgment in affirmation and negation, as will come in its verification (Sharif).
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage defines the two main categories of propositions: simple and compound. The author begins by defining the simple proposition as one whose essential meaning is either pure affirmation or pure negation. For example, 'Every human is an animal by necessity' is simple because it only affirms 'animal' of 'human' with necessity. Similarly, 'Nothing human is a stone by necessity' is simple because it only negates 'stone' from 'human'. The compound proposition, on the other hand, is composed of both affirmation and negation in its meaning. An example is 'Every human is actually a writer, not always' — this affirms writing for humans but negates its perpetuity. The author then elaborates on how a compound proposition can arise: when a judgment of affirmation is made first, then a negation is added using a dependent expression (like 'not always'), the whole counts as one compound proposition. The dependent expression indicates the modality of the original affirmative relation, and since it conveys a negative judgment, it makes the proposition compound. The author clarifies that if the negation were expressed independently, there would be two separate propositions, not one compound. Importantly, the passage notes that while every compound proposition is modal (i.e., contains a modality like 'necessarily' or 'always'), not every modal proposition is compound. Modalities like necessity or perpetuity do not introduce a second judgment; they simply qualify the single judgment. In contrast, modalities like 'not necessary' or 'not always' introduce a new judgment contrary to the first, thereby causing composition. This distinction will be verified later.

Key Terms
قضية بسيطة
إيجاب
سلب
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a simple proposition?
A) Every human is actually a writer, not always.
B) Every human is an animal by necessity.
C) Every human is laughing, not always.
D) Every human is mortal but not permanently.

Key Takeaway
A simple proposition contains exactly one judgment: either pure affirmation or pure negation.

Propositions

Compound Propositions: When Affirmation and Negation Combine
Author defines the compound proposition and elaborates on its formation using a dependent modal expression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a compound proposition and explain how a dependent modal expression can make it compound.

Core Idea
A compound proposition combines both affirmation and negation in a single judgment through a modality like 'not always'.

Some propositions seem to say one thing but actually contain two judgments. For example, 'Every human is a writer, not always' both affirms writing and negates its permanence. The author explains how such compounds arise and why they are considered one proposition rather than two separate ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two judgments
A compound proposition's reality is 'composed of affirmation and negation'. Find both within the meaning. Example: 'Every human is actually a writer, not always' affirms writing and negates it being always.
2
Spot the dependent expression
The negation is expressed through a dependent phrase like 'not always' (lā dā’iman), not an independent sentence. This keeps it as one proposition.
If the negation were independent (e.g., 'and he is not always a writer'), it would be two separate propositions.
3
Understand the role of modality
The phrase 'not always' indicates a modality (jiha): it tells how the affirmative relation holds. Because it also conveys a negative judgment, it makes the proposition compound.
Modalities like 'necessarily' or 'always' do not introduce a new judgment, so they do not create composition.
4
Contrast with simple modal propositions
Not every modal proposition is compound. For example, 'Every human is an animal by necessity' is modal but simple because 'by necessity' does not add a second judgment.

Source Text

Wording vs. Reality in Proposition Definitions
Author explains the reason for using 'reality' rather than 'wording' in defining propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the wording and the meaning of a proposition and identify when a proposition is compound in meaning despite simple wording.

Core Idea
A proposition's wording can be simple while its meaning contains hidden composition.

In defining simple and compound propositions, the author deliberately uses 'its reality' (haqiqatuha) rather than 'its wording' (lafzuha). This choice highlights a subtle but crucial point: a proposition may appear uncomplicated in its verbal form yet contain multiple judgments in its logical core. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion when analyzing modal propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's terminological choice
The author uses 'its reality' (haqiqatuha) instead of 'its wording' (lafzuha). Ask yourself: what is the key difference between these two terms? The author's reason is that a proposition may be compound in reality but not in wording.
2
Analyze the example of 'specific possibility'
Consider the proposition 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility' (كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص). Its wording contains no explicit negation or conjunction. But what does its meaning entail? It implies two things: (1) the affirmation of writing for humans is not necessary, and (2) the negation of writing for humans is not necessary.
3
Map the hidden compound meaning
The meaning is compound because it involves two modal judgments: a negative judgment (affirmation is not necessary) and a positive judgment (negation is not necessary). Even though the wording is simple, the logical content includes both a negation and an affirmation regarding necessity.
4
Contrast with explicit modal qualifiers
When we qualify a proposition with 'non-perpetuity' (لا دوام) or 'non-necessity' (لا ضرورة), the wording itself shows the negation (e.g., 'not always'). In such cases, the composition is obvious in both wording and meaning. But with possibility (imkan), the composition is hidden in meaning.
5
Apply the principle to other modal propositions
Test other examples: Is 'Every human is a writer necessarily' compound in meaning? No, it contains only one judgment. What about 'Some humans are writers possibly'? It is compound because it implies non-necessity of the universal judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قال : «حقيقتها» - أي معناها - ولم يقل : «لفظها»، لأنه ربما تكون قضيّة مركبة ولا تركيب في اللفظ من الإيجاب والسلب، كقولنا : «كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان «الخاص» فإنّه وإن لم يكن في لفظه تركيب إلا أن معناه أن إيجاب الكتابة للإنسان ليس بضروري - و هو ممكن عـام سالب - وأن سلب الكتابة عنه ليس بضروري - وهو ممكن عام موجب - فهو في الحقيقة والمعنى مركب وإن لم يوجد تركيب في اللفظ؛ بخلاف ما إذا قيدنا القضيّة باللادوام واللاضرورة، فإنّ التركيب حينئذ في القضية بحسب اللفظ أيضاً.
And he only said 'its reality' - i.e., its meaning - and he did not say 'its wording', because perhaps a proposition is compound and there is no composition in the wording from affirmation and negation, as our saying: 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility'. For although there is no composition in its wording, its meaning is that the affirmation of writing for human is not necessary - and it is a general possible negative - and that the negation of writing from him is not necessary - and it is a general possible affirmative - so it is in reality and meaning compound even if there is no composition in the wording; unlike when we qualify the proposition with non-perpetuity and non-necessity, for then the composition in the proposition is also in terms of wording.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the earlier definition, explaining why the author used 'its reality' (i.e., its meaning) rather than 'its wording' when defining simple and compound propositions. The reason is that a proposition can be compound in meaning even if its wording shows no explicit combination of affirmation and negation. The example given is 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility (imkan khas)'. The wording appears simple, but its meaning involves two judgments: the affirmation of writing is not necessary (a negative judgment in modal terms) and the negation of writing is not necessary (a positive modal judgment). Thus, in terms of reality and meaning, it is compound because it involves both a form of negation and affirmation regarding necessity. The passage contrasts this with propositions qualified by 'non-perpetuity' or 'non-necessity' (la dawam, la darura), where the composition is also reflected in the wording (e.g., 'not always' explicitly adds a negation). This highlights the subtlety that modality, especially possibility, can create a compound logical meaning without a compound linguistic expression.

Key Terms
حقيقة (ḥaqīqa): The essential meaning or logical content of a proposition, as opposed to its wording. [Note: No significant shift.]
لفظ (lafẓ): The verbal expression or wording of a proposition. [Note: No significant shift.]
ممكن خاص (mumkin khāṣṣ): Specific possibility, a modal notion that entails both non-necessity of existence and non-necessity of non-existence. [Note: In some texts, mumkin khas is distinguished from mumkin 'amm; this passage equates its meaning with two general possible judgments.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because all propositions are compound.
D) Because 'reality' is easier to translate.

Key Takeaway
The logical content of a proposition may be compound even when its verbal form is simple; modal possibility is a prime example of this phenomenon.

The Absolute Necessary Proposition
Author begins listing the 13 modal propositions, starting with the first simple: absolute necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the absolute necessary proposition and distinguish its affirmative and negative forms.

Core Idea
The absolute necessary proposition holds true for the entire duration of the subject's existence.

Modal logic classifies propositions based on the mode of judgment. The first simple modal proposition introduced is the absolute necessary (ضرورية مطلقة). This proposition asserts that a predicate is necessarily true (or false) of the subject as long as the subject exists. Understanding this foundational type is essential for mastering modal syllogisms and defining terms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining condition
The condition is that the judgment (affirmation or negation) holds for as long as the subject's essence exists (مادام ذات الموضوع موجودة). This means the truth of the proposition is coextensive with the subject's existence.
2
Distinguish affirmative and negative forms
The affirmative necessary (ضرورية موجبة) affirms the predicate necessarily, e.g., 'Every human is an animal by necessity'. The negative necessary (ضرورية سالبة) negates the predicate necessarily, e.g., 'Nothing human is a stone by necessity'.
3
Test the definition with examples
Check each example: as long as a human exists, is it necessarily an animal? Yes. Is it necessarily not a stone? Yes. Both satisfy the condition.
4
Recognize independence from accidents
The absolute necessary proposition remains true even if the subject loses accidental properties, as long as the essence remains. This distinguishes it from other modal types like perpetual or time-bound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن القضايا البسيطة والمركبة غير محصورة في عدد، إلا أن القضايا التي جرت العادة بالبحث عنها وعن أحكامها - من التناقض والعكس والقياس وغيرها - ثلاثة عشر : منها البسائط، ومنها المركبات.
أما البسائط فست
الضرورية والدائمة المطلقتين والنسبة بينهما]
الأولى : الضرورية المطلقة، وهي التي يحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع - أو بضرورة سلبه عنه - مادام ذات الموضوع موجودة. أما التي حكم فيها بضرورة الثبوت فهي ضرورية موجبة، كقولنا : «كلّ إنسان حيوان بالضرورة»، فإن الحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت الحيوان للإنسان في جميع أوقات وجوده.
وأما التي حكم فيها بضرورة السلب فضرورية سالبة، كقولنا : «لاشيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة فإنّ الحكم فيها بضرورة سلب
Then indeed the simple and compound propositions are not limited in number, except that the propositions which it is customary to investigate along with their rulings - from contradiction, conversion, syllogism, and others - are thirteen: some are simple, and some are compound.
As for the simple, they are six [the necessary and the absolute perpetual and the relation between them?]
The first: the absolute necessary, which is that in which one judges with necessity of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject - or with necessity of its negation from it - as long as the essence of the subject exists. As for that in which one judges with necessity of affirmation, it is a necessary affirmative, as our saying: 'Every human is an animal by necessity'; for the judgment in it is with necessity of affirmation of animal to human in all times of his existence.
And as for that in which one judges with necessity of negation, it is a necessary negative, as our saying: 'Nothing of human is a stone by necessity'; for the judgment in it is with necessity of negation [sentence incomplete]
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage begins by stating that while simple and compound propositions are not limited in number, logicians customarily study thirteen specific types due to their importance in rules of contradiction, conversion, and syllogism. These thirteen include both simple and compound propositions. The passage then starts enumerating the six simple propositions, beginning with the first: the absolute necessary proposition (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa). This proposition judges with necessity that the predicate belongs to the subject (or does not belong) as long as the subject's essence exists. The author provides two examples: the affirmative necessary ('Every human is an animal by necessity') and the negative necessary ('Nothing human is a stone by necessity'). In the affirmative, the judgment is that animality is necessarily affirmed of human at all times of his existence. In the negative, the judgment is that stoneness is necessarily negated of human. The passage breaks off mid-sentence while describing the negative case. The enumeration is part of a larger classification typical of works on modal logic.

ضرورية موجبة
ضرورية سالبة

Key Takeaway
The absolute necessary proposition is defined by the condition that its necessity holds for the entire duration of the subject's existence.

?
Identify which of the following is an absolute necessary proposition: 'Some humans are writers by accident' or 'Every human is rational by necessity'. Justify your answer using the condition 'as long as the subject exists'.

Absolute Necessary vs Absolute Perpetual
Author defines first two simple modal propositions and explains their logical relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the absolute necessary and the absolute perpetual propositions and explain why the necessary is more specific.

Core Idea
Necessity implies perpetuity, but perpetuity does not imply necessity.

In Islamic modal logic, the manner of judgment (kayfiyya) classifies propositions. The two fundamental simple modal types are the absolute necessary (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa) and the absolute perpetual (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa). Understanding their definitions and the logical relationship between them is crucial for evaluating demonstrations (burhan) and dialectical arguments in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposition types
Locate the definitions of the absolute necessary and absolute perpetual in the passage. Note the key phrases: 'judgment with necessity' vs 'judgment with perpetuity'.
The Arabic terms are الضرورية المطلقة and الدائمة المطلقة.
2
Extract the defining condition for each
For the necessary, the condition is 'impossibility of separation' (imtina' al-infikak) of the predicate from the subject. For the perpetual, the condition is 'universal temporal coverage' (dawam al-azmina) — the predicate holds at all times the subject exists.
Necessity is a stronger condition because it denies the possibility of separation at any moment.
3
Apply the examples
Review the examples: 'Every human is an animal by necessity' shows necessity because animality cannot separate from humanity. 'Always every human is an animal' shows perpetuity because at every time a human exists, it is an animal. Note that the same example can serve both, but the necessity version adds the impossibility of separation.
The perpetual example does not explicitly state impossibility, only actual continuous truth.
4
Analyze the relationship
The author states: 'the necessary is more specific than it absolutely' (أخص منها مطلقاً). This means necessity entails perpetuity: if a relation cannot be separated, it must hold at all times. But perpetuity does not entail necessity: a relation could hold at all times even though separation is possible (e.g., 'Every human breathes' perpetually but not necessarily).
Use the concept of imkan (possibility): a possible separation that never occurs does not negate perpetuity.
5
Verify with an Islamic example
Consider 'Every human is created'. Is this necessary or perpetual? Creation is inseparable from humanity (necessary). Now consider 'Every human speaks'. This is perpetual (true at all times for humans) but not necessary, as speech could be ceased (e.g., a mute human is still human).
This distinction is vital in kalam for arguments about divine attributes and temporal origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن القضايا البسيطة والمركبة غير محصورة في عدد، إلا أن القضايا التي جرت العادة بالبحث عنها وعن أحكامها - من التناقض والعكس والقياس وغيرها - ثلاثة عشر : منها البسائط، ومنها المركبات.
أما البسائط فست
الضرورية والدائمة المطلقتين والنسبة بينهما]
الأولى : الضرورية المطلقة، وهي التي يحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع - أو بضرورة سلبه عنه - مادام ذات الموضوع موجودة. أما التي حكم فيها بضرورة الثبوت فهي ضرورية موجبة، كقولنا : «كلّ إنسان حيوان بالضرورة»، فإن الحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت الحيوان للإنسان في جميع أوقات وجوده.
وأما التي حكم فيها بضرورة السلب فضرورية سالبة، كقولنا : «لاشيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة فإنّ الحكم فيها بضرورة سلب الحجرية عن الإنسان في جميع أوقات وجوده، وإنما سميت «ضرورية» لاشتمالها على الضرورة، و «مطلقة» لعدم تقييد الضرورة فيها بوصف أو وقت.
الثانية : الدائمة المطلقة، وهي التي حكم فيها بدوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع - أو بدوام سلبه عنه - مادام ذات الموضوع موجودة، ووجه تسميتها دائمة ومطلقة على قياس الضرورية المطلقة، ومثالها إيجاباً ما مر من قولنا : «دائماً كلّ إنسان حيوان فقد حكمنا فيها بدوام ثبوت الحيوانية للإنسان مادام ذاته موجودة - وسلباً ما مر أيضاً من قولنا : دائماً لاشيء من الإنسان بحجر » - فإن الحكم فيها بدوام سلب الحجرية عن الإنسان مادام ذاته موجودة.
والنسبة بينها وبين الضرورية أن الضرورية أخص منها مطلقاً، لأن مفهوم الضرورة امتناع انفكاك النسبة عن الموضوع، ومفهوم الدوام شمول الأزمنة والأوقات ومتى كانت النسبة ممتنعة الانفكاك عن النسبة في جميع الموضوع كانت متحققة في جميع أوقات وجوده بالضرورة، وليس متى كانت النسبة متحققة في جميع الأوقات امتنع انفكاكها عن الموضوع، لجواز إمكان انفكاكها عن الموضوع وعدم وقوعه، لأن الممكن لا يجب أن يكون واقعاً.
Then indeed the simple and compound propositions are not limited in number, except that the propositions which it is customary to investigate and study their rules — of contradiction, conversion, syllogism, and others — are thirteen: among them are simple, and among them are compound. As for the simple ones, they are six: The first: the absolute necessary [proposition], which is that wherein judgment is made with necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject — or with necessity of its negation from it — as long as the essence of the subject exists. As for that wherein judgment is made with necessity of affirmation, it is a necessary affirmative, like our saying: 'Every human is an animal by necessity,' for the judgment therein is with necessity of animal's affirmation for the human in all times of its existence. And as for that wherein judgment is made with necessity of negation, it is a necessary negative, like our saying: 'No human is a stone by necessity,' for the judgment therein is with necessity of stoneness's negation from the human in all times of its existence. It is called 'necessary' for its inclusion of necessity, and 'absolute' for the lack of restriction of necessity by a description or time. The second: the absolute perpetual [proposition], which is that wherein judgment is made with perpetuity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject — or with perpetuity of its negation from it — as long as the essence of the subject exists. The reason for its being called perpetual and absolute is analogous to the absolute necessary. Its example in affirmation is what has passed in our saying: 'Always every human is an animal,' for we have judged therein with perpetuity of animality's affirmation for the human as long as its essence exists; and in negation, what has also passed in our saying: 'Always no human is a stone,' for the judgment therein is with perpetuity of stoneness's negation from the human as long as its essence exists. And the relationship between it and the necessary is that the necessary is more specific than it absolutely, because the concept of necessity is the impossibility of the separation of the relation from the subject, and the concept of perpetuity is the encompassing of times and moments. Whenever the relation is impossible to separate from the relation in all subjects, it is realized in all times of its existence necessarily; but it is not the case that whenever the relation is realized in all times, its separation from the subject is impossible, for it is possible that its separation from the subject is possible but does not occur, because what is possible does not have to be actual.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes the first two types of simple modal propositions in Islamic logic: the absolute necessary (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa) and the absolute perpetual (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa). The author begins by noting that while propositions (qadaya) are unlimited, logicians conventionally study thirteen types—six simple and seven compound—for their logical properties like contradiction, conversion, and syllogism. He then specifies the first simple one: the absolute necessary. This is a proposition where the judgment (either affirmative or negative) is made with necessity as long as the subject's essence exists. The example given is 'Every human is an animal by necessity' — this is necessary because human cannot exist without being animal; the relation is inseparable. Similarly, 'No human is a stone by necessity' is also necessary because the negation is inseparable. It is called 'necessary' because it includes the modality of necessity, and 'absolute' because the necessity is not restricted to a specific description or time. The second type is the absolute perpetual, where the judgment holds for all times (perpetually) as long as the subject exists, but without the condition of necessity. So 'Always every human is an animal' is perpetual because at every moment of human existence, it is animal, but it is also necessary. However, perpetuity does not entail necessity; the author then explains the logical relationship (al-nisba) between the two: necessity is a special case of perpetuity (a'akhass). The concept of necessity involves the impossibility of separation (imtina' infikak), while perpetuity only involves universal temporal coverage. Therefore, if a relation is necessary, it is also perpetual (because if it cannot be separated, it must hold at all times), but the converse is not true: a relation may hold at all times even though it is possible for it to separate in principle. For example, the statement 'Every human breathes' may be perpetually true (at all times humans breathe) but it is not necessary because breathing could be ceased. The author closes by noting that what is possible does not necessarily have to occur; actuality does not imply necessity. This distinction is crucial in Islamic logic for evaluating the strength of propositions used in demonstrations.

الدائمة المطلقة
الضرورة
الدوام
الإمكان

A) They are equivalent in meaning.
B) The perpetual is more specific than the necessary.
C) The necessary is more specific than the perpetual.
D) There is no logical relationship between them.

Key Takeaway
The absolute necessary proposition is more specific than the absolute perpetual because necessity entails impossibility of separation, which automatically guarantees perpetuity, but perpetual truth does not guarantee necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Defining the Absolute Perpetual Proposition
Author defines the absolute perpetual proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an absolute perpetual proposition by its definition and give examples.

Core Idea
Perpetual proposition: predicate holds as long as subject exists, no necessity required.

In this passage, the author defines the absolute perpetual proposition as one where the predicate is affirmed or denied of the subject at every moment the subject exists. This is the second type of modal proposition. We will examine its definition and examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined. The author calls it 'the absolute perpetual (al-dā'ima al-muṭlaqa)'. Note that it is the second type.
2
Extract the definiens
The definition is: 'judgment with perpetuity of affirmation or negation of the predicate to the subject as long as the essence of the subject exists.' Identify the key components: perpetuity, subject existence, predicate affirmation/negation.
3
Examine the examples
The author gives two examples: affirmative 'Always, every human is an animal' and negative 'Always, nothing of human is a stone'. Note that these are perpetual because they hold at all times humans exist.
4
Understand the terminology
The term 'perpetual' refers to the time condition (duration of subject's existence), and 'absolute' means without any additional condition such as necessity or time qualification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية : الدائمة المطلقة، وهي التي حكم فيها بدوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع - أو بدوام سلبه عنه - مادام ذات الموضوع موجودة، ووجه تسميتها دائمة ومطلقة على قياس الضرورية المطلقة، ومثالها إيجاباً ما مر من قولنا : «دائماً كلّ إنسان حيوان فقد حكمنا فيها بدوام ثبوت الحيوانية للإنسان مادام ذاته موجودة - وسلباً ما مر أيضاً من قولنا : دائماً لاشيء من الإنسان بحجر » - فإن الحكم فيها بدوام سلب الحجرية عن الإنسان مادام ذاته موجودة.
والنسبة بينها وبين الضرورية أن الضرورية أخص منها مطلقاً، لأن مفهوم الضرورة امتناع انفكاك النسبة عن الموضوع، ومفهوم الدوام شمول الأزمنة والأوقات ومتى كانت النسبة ممتنعة الانفكاك عن النسبة في جميع الموضوع كانت متحققة في جميع أوقات وجوده بالضرورة، وليس متى كانت النسبة متحققة في جميع الأوقات امتنع انفكاكها عن الموضوع، لجواز إمكان انفكاكها عن الموضوع وعدم وقوعه، لأن الممكن لا يجب أن يكون واقعاً.
The second: the absolute perpetual (al-dā'ima al-muṭlaqa), which is that wherein it is judged with perpetuity of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject—or with perpetuity of its negation from it—as long as the essence of the subject exists. The reason for its being called 'perpetual' and 'absolute' is by analogy with the absolute necessary. Its example affirmatively is what has passed of our saying: 'Always, every human is an animal'—for we have judged therein with the perpetuity of the affirmation of animality to the human as long as his essence exists. And negatively also what has passed of our saying: 'Always, nothing of human is a stone'—for the judgment therein is with the perpetuity of the negation of stoneness from the human as long as his essence exists.
And the relation between it and the necessary is that the necessary is more specific than it absolutely, because the concept of necessity is the impossibility of the separation of the relation from the subject, and the concept of perpetuity is the encompassment of times and moments. Whenever the relation is impossible to separate from the subject in all subjects, it is realized in all times of its existence necessarily. But it is not the case that whenever the relation is realized in all times, its separation from the subject is impossible, because it is permissible that its separation from the subject be possible yet does not occur, for the possible does not have to be actual.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of modal proposition, the absolute perpetual (al-dā'ima al-muṭlaqa). The author explains that it is a judgment of perpetual affirmation or negation of the predicate for the subject, as long as the subject's essence exists. The term 'perpetual' indicates duration over all times, and 'absolute' indicates no additional condition like necessity. Examples are given: affirmative 'always every human is an animal' and negative 'always nothing of human is a stone'. Then the author compares it to the necessary proposition (al-ḍarūriyya). The necessary is more specific: if a relation is necessary (impossible to separate), it is automatically perpetual (true at all times). But the converse is not true: a relation can be perpetually true without being necessary, because it could be possible for it to separate but it never actually does. For instance, 'every human is an animal' is both necessary and perpetual because animality is essential. However, consider 'every human is laughing'—if it were always true while humans exist, it would be perpetual but not necessary, since laughter is accidental. The author emphasizes that possibility does not necessitate actuality.

Distinguishing Perpetual from Necessary: Why Necessity Implies Perpetuity but Not Vice Versa
Author compares perpetual and necessary propositions, establishing that necessary is more specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logical relationship between perpetual and necessary propositions and provide a counterexample where a proposition is perpetual but not necessary.

Core Idea
Necessity entails perpetuity, but perpetuity does not entail necessity.

After defining the absolute perpetual proposition, the author compares it to the absolute necessary. He explains that necessity is a stronger condition: if a relation is necessary, it is automatically perpetual, but the converse is not true. This is a crucial distinction in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types being compared
The author compares the absolute perpetual (al-dā'ima al-muṭlaqa) and the absolute necessary (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa). Note that both are 'absolute' (without condition), but one adds necessity.
2
Understand the direction of implication
The author states that the necessary is 'more specific' (akhaṣṣ), meaning that every necessary proposition is perpetual, but not every perpetual is necessary. This is because necessity means impossibility of separation, while perpetuity only means actual occurrence at all times.
3
Grasp the logical reason
The author explains: if a relation is impossible to separate, it must be true at all times (necessary -> perpetual). But if it is always true, it could still be possible for it to separate without ever actually separating (possible but not actual). So perpetual does not imply necessary.
4
Construct a counterexample
Think of a proposition that is always true but could be false without contradiction. For example: 'Every human is a speaking being' (nāṭiq) if all humans speak, but it is possible for a human to be mute. So it would be perpetual but not necessary.
The passage does not give this example, but it follows from the logic.

General Conditional Proposition (al-Mashrūṭa al-'Āmma)
Author defines the general conditional proposition and gives two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the general conditional proposition and distinguish its two interpretations.

Core Idea
The general conditional ties necessity to a description that influences the judgment.

In the study of modal propositions, the general conditional introduces a condition upon which the necessity of the judgment depends. This passage defines it and then presents a subtle difference in how that condition is understood. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing conditional statements in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and core definition
Locate the term 'المشروطة العامة' in the Arabic text and extract the definition: a proposition that judges with necessity of affirmation or negation of the predicate to the subject, on condition that the subject is characterized by a specific description, and that description has an influence in the necessity.
2
Analyze the examples given
Examine the affirmative example (every writer is a finger-mover necessarily as long as he is a writer) and the negative example (nothing of the writer is finger-still as long as he is a writer). Note that the necessity is only when the description (writing) is present, and the description causes the necessity.
The examples show that without the description, the judgment is not necessary.
3
Extract the two interpretations
The author notes an alternative definition: the general conditional is a proposition where the judgment is necessary in all times the description subsists, regardless of whether the description causally influences the necessity. Distinguish this from the first interpretation where the description must have influence.
The difference is whether the necessity is relative to the combined essence+description (first) or simply during the duration of the description (second).
4
Summarize the naming rationale
It is called 'conditional' because it includes a condition (description) and 'general' because it is more general than the specific conditional (to be discussed later).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة : المشروطة العامة وهي التي حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه بشرط أن يكون ذات الموضوع متصفاً بوصف الموضوع، أي يكون لوصف الموضوع دخل في تحقق الضرورة.
مثال الموجبة قولنا : كلّ كاتب متحرك الأصابع بالضرورة مادام كاتباً، فإنّ تحرّك الأصابع ليس بضروري الثبوت لذات الكاتب – أعني أفراد الإنسان مطلقاً - بل ضرورة ثبوته إنما هي بشرط اتصافها بوصف الكتابة.
ومثال السالبة قولنا : بالضرورة لاشيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع مادام كاتباً، فإن سلب ساكن الأصابع عن ذات الكاتب ليس بضروري إلا بشرط اتصافها بالكتابة.
وسبب تسميتها أما بالمشروطة : فلاشتمالها على شرط الوصف؛ وأما بالعامة فلأنّها أعم من المشروطة الخاصة - وستعرفها في المركبات.
وربما يقال : «المشروطة العامة على القضيّة التي حكم فيها بضرورة الثبوت أو بضرورة السلب في جميع أوقات ثبوت الوصف، أعم من أن يكون للوصف مدخل في تحقق الضرورة، أم لا ؛ والفرق بين المعنيين )
(۱) حاصله أن المشروطة إذا اعتبرت بشرط الوصف كان ضرورة نسبة المحمول - إيجاباً أو سلباً - بالقياس إلى ذات الموضوع مأخوذاً مع وصفه - فالضرورة إنما هي بالقياس إلى مجموع الذات والوصف - وإذا اعتبرت مادام
The third: the general conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-'āmma), which is that wherein it is judged with the necessity of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject or its negation from it, on condition that the essence of the subject be characterized by a description of the subject, i.e., the description of the subject has an influence in the realization of the necessity.
An example of the affirmative is our saying: 'Every writer is a finger-mover necessarily as long as he is a writer.' For the movement of the fingers is not a necessary affirmation for the essence of a writer—I mean the individuals of human absolute—rather the necessity of its affirmation is only on condition of its being characterized by the description of writing.
And an example of the negative is our saying: 'Necessarily, nothing of the writer is finger-still as long as he is a writer.' For the negation of finger-stillness from the essence of a writer is not necessary except on condition of its being characterized by writing.
The reason for its naming: as for 'conditional' (mashrūṭa), it is because it includes a condition of description; and as for 'general' ('āmma), it is because it is more general than the specific conditional—and you will know it in the compounds.
And it may be said: 'The general conditional is the proposition wherein it is judged with the necessity of affirmation or the necessity of negation in all times of the subsistence of the description, regardless of whether the description has an influence in the realization of the necessity or not. And the difference between the two meanings... (1)
Its summary: that the conditional, if considered with the condition of the description, then the necessity of the relation of the predicate—affirmatively or negatively—in relation to the essence of the subject taken together with its description—so the necessity is only in relation to the sum of the essence and the description. And if considered as long as...
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of modal proposition, the general conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-'āmma). The author explains that it judges the predicate to be necessarily true or false for the subject, but only under the condition that the subject is characterized by a specific description (waṣf). That description has an influence in bringing about the necessity. For example, 'every writer is a finger-mover necessarily as long as he is a writer'—the movement of fingers is not necessary for the human essence, but only necessary when the person is actually writing. Similarly, 'nothing of the writer is finger-still as long as he is a writer'—the stillness is negated necessarily only during writing. The name 'conditional' comes from including a condition, and 'general' because it is more general than the specific conditional (which will be discussed later). The author then notes an alternative interpretation: some define the general conditional as a proposition where the judgment is necessary in all times the description subsists, regardless of whether the description causally influences the necessity. The difference between the two interpretations is summarized: in the first, necessity is relative to the combined essence+description; in the second, it is simply during the duration of the description. The passage ends mid-explanation.

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة
وصف الموضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a general conditional proposition according to the first interpretation (description must influence necessity)?
A) Every human is mortal necessarily.
B) Every worshiper in prayer is forbidden from speaking necessarily as long as he is in prayer.
C) Every animal moves by itself.
D) Nothing of the scholar is ignorant at all times.

Key Takeaway
The general conditional proposition is distinguished by having a condition (description) that causes the necessity of the judgment, and there are two interpretations regarding whether the description must causally influence the necessity or merely accompany it temporally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a general conditional proposition in the affirmative on a topic from Islamic law (e.g., fasting, prayer, zakat). Ensure the description (waṣf) is mentioned and has an influence on the necessity of the judgment. Write both the proposition and explain why it fits the first interpretation.
?
Compare the two interpretations of the general conditional using your own Islamic example. Show how the necessity is understood differently in each interpretation.

6 min
The General Conditional: Definition and Examples
Defines the general conditional and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a general conditional proposition and explain how it differs from absolute propositions by requiring a descriptive condition for necessity.

Core Idea
Necessity is not for the essence but for the essence qualified by a description.

The general conditional is a modal proposition where the predicate is necessary for the subject only when the subject is described by a certain attribute. In this lesson, you will learn its formal definition, its naming, and concrete examples from logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Locate the term being defined: 'المشروطة العامة' (general conditional). Note that it is the third type of modal proposition.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
Genus: a proposition that judges necessity of a predicate for a subject. Differentia: the necessity is conditioned on the subject being described by a specific description, and that description has an input in the necessity.
The condition is 'bi-shart al-wasf' – the description is part of what the necessity is attributed to.
3
Analyze the examples
The affirmative example: 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer.' The negative: 'No writer is still-fingered necessarily as long as he is a writer.' Note that the necessity holds only under the description 'writer'.
The predicate (moving fingers) is not necessary for the essence 'human' but only when qualified by writing.
4
Understand the naming
Why 'conditional' (mashruta)? Because it includes a condition (the description). Why 'general' ('amma)? Because it is broader than the 'special conditional' to be discussed later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة : المشروطة العامة وهي التي حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه بشرط أن يكون ذات الموضوع متصفاً بوصف الموضوع، أي يكون لوصف الموضوع دخل في تحقق الضرورة. مثال الموجبة قولنا : كلّ كاتب متحرك الأصابع بالضرورة مادام كاتباً، فإنّ تحرّك الأصابع ليس بضروري الثبوت لذات الكاتب – أعني أفراد الإنسان مطلقاً - بل ضرورة ثبوته إنما هي بشرط اتصافها بوصف الكتابة. ومثال السالبة قولنا : بالضرورة لاشيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع مادام كاتباً، فإن سلب ساكن الأصابع عن ذات الكاتب ليس بضروري إلا بشرط اتصافها بالكتابة. وسبب تسميتها أما بالمشروطة : فلاشتمالها على شرط الوصف؛ وأما بالعامة فلأنّها أعم من المشروطة الخاصة - وستعرفها في المركبات. وربما يقال : «المشروطة العامة على القضيّة التي حكم فيها بضرورة الثبوت أو بضرورة السلب في جميع أوقات ثبوت الوصف، أعم من أن يكون للوصف مدخل في تحقق الضرورة، أم لا ؛ والفرق بين المعنيين حاصله أن المشروطة إذا اعتبرت بشرط الوصف كان ضرورة نسبة المحمول - إيجاباً أو سلباً - بالقياس إلى ذات الموضوع مأخوذاً مع وصفه - فالضرورة إنما هي بالقياس إلى مجموع الذات والوصف - وإذا اعتبرت مادام الوصف كان الوصف هناك معتبراً على أنه ظرف للضرورة، لاجزء لمانسب إليه الضرورة، وإلا لزم اعتبار الوصف مرتين : مرة جزء لما نسب إليه الضرورة، ومرة ظرفاً للضرورة، فيصير المعنى أن نسبة المحمول ضرورية لمجموع ذات الموضوع مع وصفه في جميع أوقات وصفه، ولا فائدة لاعتبار الظرف هاهنا. فتعين أنه إذا اعتبرت مادام الوصف كان ضرورة نسبة المحمول إلى ذات الموضوع فقط، وحينئذ إن لم يكن الوصف الذي له مدخل في تحقق الضرورة ضرورياً لذات الموضوع حال ثبوته له - كالكتابة – صدقت المشروطة بشرط الوصف - دون مادام الوصف - وإن كان ضرورياً له في زمان ثبوتـه لـه صدقت المشروطة بالمعنيين معاً كقولك : كلّ منخسف فهو مظلم مادام منخسفاً، سواء أريد منه بشرط كونه منخسفاً أو مادام منخسفاً بلا اعتبار الاشتراط، بناء على أن الانحساف ضروري للقمر في وقت معين – وهو وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس - فإن نسبة الإظلام إلى مجموع القمر ووصف الانحساف كان ضرورياً له، وإن نسبته إلى ذات القمر، كان أيضاً ضرورياً له في وقت الانحساف ؛ لأن القمر في ذلك الوقت يستحيل وجوده بلا انحساف على - مازعموا - فذات القمر مستلزم للمجموع من ذاته ووصف الإنخساف، وهذا المجموع مستلزم للإظلام، ومستلزم المستلزم مستلزم، فذات القمر في ذلك الوقت مستلزم للإظلام ؛ فظهر بذلك أن النسبة بين معنيي المشروطة هي العموم من وجه. وهذا الكلام ، محقق، وقد أخطأ فيه كثيرون، وزعموا أن النسبة بينهما العموم مطلقاً، لأن مادام الوصف أعم مطلقاً.
The third: the general conditional, which is a judgment in which there is a judgment of the necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject or its negation from it, on condition that the subject's essence is described by the description of the subject, i.e., the description of the subject has an input in the realization of the necessity. An example of the affirmative is our statement: 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer,' for the movement of the fingers is not a necessary affirmation for the essence of the writer – I mean the individuals of man absolutely – but rather its necessity of affirmation is only on condition of their being described by the description of writing. An example of the negative is our statement: 'Necessarily, no writer is still-fingered as long as he is a writer,' for the negation of still-fingeredness from the essence of the writer is not necessary except on condition of their being described by writing. The reason for its naming: as for 'conditional' (mashruta), it is because it includes a condition of description; as for 'general' ('amma), it is because it is more general than the special conditional – and you will know it in the compound propositions. And it is sometimes said: 'The general conditional is the proposition in which there is a judgment of necessity of affirmation or necessity of negation in all times of the description's existence, regardless of whether the description has an input in the realization of necessity or not; and the difference between the two meanings is that if the conditional is considered with the condition of description, then the necessity of the predicate's relation – affirmation or negation – is relative to the essence of the subject taken together with its description – so necessity is only relative to the aggregate of essence and description – and if it is considered as long as (mada) the description, then the description there is considered as a circumstance for the necessity, not a part of what the necessity is attributed to, otherwise it would necessitate considering the description twice: once as a part of what the necessity is attributed to, and once as a circumstance for the necessity, so the meaning would become that the predicate's relation is necessary for the aggregate of the subject's essence and its description in all times of its description, and there is no benefit to considering the circumstance here. So it is determined that if it is considered as long as the description, then the necessity of the predicate's relation is only to the subject's essence alone, and then if the description that has an input in realizing the necessity is not necessary for the subject's essence at the time of its existence for it – like writing – then the conditional with the condition of description is true, but not the conditional as long as the description. And if it is necessary for it at the time of its existence for it, then the conditional is true with both meanings together, like your statement: 'Every eclipsed moon is dark as long as it is eclipsed,' whether it is meant with the condition of being eclipsed or as long as it is eclipsed without considering the condition, based on the fact that the eclipse is necessary for the moon at a specific time – which is the time of the Earth's interposition between it and the sun – for the relation of darkness to the aggregate of the moon and the description of eclipse was necessary for it, and its relation to the essence of the moon was also necessary for it at the time of eclipse; because the moon at that time is impossible to exist without the eclipse, according to their claim – so the essence of the moon entails the aggregate of its essence and the description of eclipse, and this aggregate entails darkness, and the necessary of the necessary is necessary, so the essence of the moon at that time entails darkness. So it appears from that that the relation between the two meanings of the conditional is 'general and specific in some respects' ('umum min wajh). And this statement is verified, and many have erred in it, and they claimed that the relation between them is absolute generality, because 'as long as the description' is absolutely more general.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of modal proposition, the 'general conditional' (al-mashrutat al-'amma), and then clarifies a subtle distinction between two of its interpretations. It begins by giving the standard definition: a proposition that asserts the necessity of the predicate's affirmation or negation for the subject, on the condition that the subject's essence is described by that description. In other words, the description is essential for the necessity. The affirmative example is 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer.' The movement is not necessary for the essence 'writer' (i.e., human being), but only necessary when qualified by writing. The negative example is 'No writer is still-fingered necessarily as long as he is a writer.' The text then explains that the proposition is named 'conditional' because it includes a condition (the description), and 'general' because it is broader than the 'special conditional' (to be defined later). Next, the text introduces an alternative definition cited from others, which says the general conditional is a judgment of necessity in all times of the description's existence, regardless of whether the description causes the necessity. The difference between the two definitions is crucial: In the first (condition-of-description), the necessity is relative to the subject's essence taken with its description—the description is part of what the necessity is attributed to. In the second (as-long-as-the-description), the description is merely a circumstance (zarf) for the necessity, not part of the subject. The text argues that if we consider the description as both part of the subject and as circumstance, we would treat it twice, which is circular and useless. Thus, only the first interpretation makes sense when the description is not necessary for the subject (e.g., writing is not a necessary attribute of humans). When the description is necessary for the subject at that time (e.g., the eclipse is necessary for the moon at the time of interposition), both interpretations agree, as shown by the eclipse example. The text concludes that the relationship between the two meanings is 'general and specific in some respects' ('umum min wajh), meaning they overlap but neither is absolutely more general. Many scholars mistakenly thought that 'as long as the description' is absolutely more general, but the author corrects this.

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة
بشرط الوصف
مادام الوصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is the proposition 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer' an example of the general conditional?
A) Because it asserts that all writers move their fingers at all times
B) Because the necessity of moving fingers is conditional on the subject being described as a writer
C) Because it contains the phrase 'as long as'
D) Because it is a universal affirmative proposition

Key Takeaway
The general conditional makes necessity depend on a specific description of the subject, not on the subject's bare essence.

Propositions

Two Interpretations: Condition vs. Circumstance
Distinguishes the two interpretations and corrects a common error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish 'condition of description' (bi-shart al-wasf) from 'as long as the description' (mada al-wasf) and determine when each applies.

Core Idea
The description can be integral to the subject or a mere circumstance, and this choice changes the truth conditions of the proposition.

The general conditional has two interpretations: one where the description is part of what the necessity is attributed to, and another where the description is just the time frame. This lesson unpacks the difference using the eclipse example and corrects a common error about their logical relationship.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two interpretations
Interpretation 1 (bi-shart al-wasf): necessity is attributed to the subject's essence taken together with its description. Interpretation 2 (mada al-wasf): necessity is attributed to the subject's essence alone, and the description is merely a circumstance (zarf) indicating when the necessity holds.
The text argues that mixing the two would treat the description twice (as part and as circumstance), which is useless.
2
Apply the eclipse example
Consider: 'Every eclipsed moon is dark as long as it is eclipsed.' The description 'eclipsed' is necessary for the moon at that time (the moon cannot exist without eclipse then). So the proposition is true under both interpretations.
When the description is not necessary for the subject (e.g., writing for humans), only the first interpretation holds.
3
Analyze the relationship between the two interpretations
The text concludes the relationship is 'general and specific in some respects' ('umum min wajh): they overlap in cases where the description is necessary for the subject, but differ otherwise.
This corrects the mistaken view that mada al-wasf is absolutely more general.
4
Recognize the common error
Many scholars incorrectly claimed mada al-wasf is absolutely more general. The text refutes this by showing cases where only bi-shart al-wasf is true (e.g., writer example).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

كالكتابة – صدقت المشروطة بشرط الوصف - دون مادام الوصف - وإن كان ضرورياً له في زمان ثبوتـه لـه صدقت المشروطة بالمعنيين معاً كقولك : كلّ منخسف فهو مظلم مادام منخسفاً، سواء أريد منه بشرط كونه منخسفاً أو مادام منخسفاً بلا اعتبار الاشتراط، بناء على أن الانحساف ضروري للقمر في وقت معين – وهو وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس - فإن نسبة الإظلام إلى مجموع القمر ووصف الانحساف كان ضرورياً له، وإن نسبته إلى ذات القمر، كان أيضاً ضرورياً له في وقت الانحساف ؛ لأن القمر في ذلك الوقت يستحيل وجوده بلا انحساف على - مازعموا - فذات القمر مستلزم للمجموع من ذاته ووصف الإنخساف، وهذا المجموع مستلزم للإظلام، ومستلزم المستلزم مستلزم، فذات القمر في ذلك الوقت مستلزم للإظلام ؛ فظهر بذلك أن النسبة بين معنيي المشروطة هي العموم من وجه. وهذا الكلام ، محقق، وقد أخطأ فيه كثيرون، وزعموا أن النسبة بينهما العموم مطلقاً، لأن مادام الوصف أعم مطلقاً.
The third: the general conditional, which is a judgment in which there is a judgment of the necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject or its negation from it, on condition that the subject's essence is described by the description of the subject, i.e., the description of the subject has an input in the realization of the necessity. An example of the affirmative is our statement: 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer,' for the movement of the fingers is not a necessary affirmation for the essence of the writer – I mean the individuals of man absolutely – but rather its necessity of affirmation is only on condition of their being described by the description of writing. An example of the negative is our statement: 'Necessarily, no writer is still-fingered as long as he is a writer,' for the negation of still-fingeredness from the essence of the writer is not necessary except on condition of their being described by writing. The reason for its naming: as for 'conditional' (mashruta), it is because it includes a condition of description; as for 'general' ('amma), it is because it is more general than the special conditional – and you will know it in the compound propositions. And it is sometimes said: 'The general conditional is the proposition in which there is a judgment of necessity of affirmation or necessity of negation in all times of the description's existence, regardless of whether the description has an input in the realization of necessity or not; and the difference between the two meanings is that if the conditional is considered with the condition of description, then the necessity of the predicate's relation – affirmation or negation – is relative to the essence of the subject taken together with its description – so necessity is only relative to the aggregate of essence and description – and if it is considered as long as (mada) the description, then the description there is considered as a circumstance for the necessity, not a part of what the necessity is attributed to, otherwise it would necessitate considering the description twice: once as a part of what the necessity is attributed to, and once as a circumstance for the necessity, so the meaning would become that the predicate's relation is necessary for the aggregate of the subject's essence and its description in all times of its description, and there is no benefit to considering the circumstance here. So it is determined that if it is considered as long as the description, then the necessity of the predicate's relation is only to the subject's essence alone, and then if the description that has an input in realizing the necessity is not necessary for the subject's essence at the time of its existence for it – like writing – then the conditional with the condition of description is true, but not the conditional as long as the description. And if it is necessary for it at the time of its existence for it, then the conditional is true with both meanings together, like your statement: 'Every eclipsed moon is dark as long as it is eclipsed,' whether it is meant with the condition of being eclipsed or as long as it is eclipsed without considering the condition, based on the fact that the eclipse is necessary for the moon at a specific time – which is the time of the Earth's interposition between it and the sun – for the relation of darkness to the aggregate of the moon and the description of eclipse was necessary for it, and its relation to the essence of the moon was also necessary for it at the time of eclipse; because the moon at that time is impossible to exist without the eclipse, according to their claim – so the essence of the moon entails the aggregate of its essence and the description of eclipse, and this aggregate entails darkness, and the necessary of the necessary is necessary, so the essence of the moon at that time entails darkness. So it appears from that that the relation between the two meanings of the conditional is 'general and specific in some respects' ('umum min wajh). And this statement is verified, and many have erred in it, and they claimed that the relation between them is absolute generality, because 'as long as the description' is absolutely more general.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of modal proposition, the 'general conditional' (al-mashrutat al-'amma), and then clarifies a subtle distinction between two of its interpretations. It begins by giving the standard definition: a proposition that asserts the necessity of the predicate's affirmation or negation for the subject, on the condition that the subject's essence is described by that description. In other words, the description is essential for the necessity. The affirmative example is 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer.' The movement is not necessary for the essence 'writer' (i.e., human being), but only necessary when qualified by writing. The negative example is 'No writer is still-fingered necessarily as long as he is a writer.' The text then explains that the proposition is named 'conditional' because it includes a condition (the description), and 'general' because it is broader than the 'special conditional' (to be defined later). Next, the text introduces an alternative definition cited from others, which says the general conditional is a judgment of necessity in all times of the description's existence, regardless of whether the description causes the necessity. The difference between the two definitions is crucial: In the first (condition-of-description), the necessity is relative to the subject's essence taken with its description—the description is part of what the necessity is attributed to. In the second (as-long-as-the-description), the description is merely a circumstance (zarf) for the necessity, not part of the subject. The text argues that if we consider the description as both part of the subject and as circumstance, we would treat it twice, which is circular and useless. Thus, only the first interpretation makes sense when the description is not necessary for the subject (e.g., writing is not a necessary attribute of humans). When the description is necessary for the subject at that time (e.g., the eclipse is necessary for the moon at the time of interposition), both interpretations agree, as shown by the eclipse example. The text concludes that the relationship between the two meanings is 'general and specific in some respects' ('umum min wajh), meaning they overlap but neither is absolutely more general. Many scholars mistakenly thought that 'as long as the description' is absolutely more general, but the author corrects this.

Key Terms
بشرط الوصف
مادام الوصف
عموم من وجه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the logical relationship between the two interpretations (bi-shart al-wasf and mada al-wasf)?
A) Mada al-wasf is absolutely more general than bi-shart al-wasf
B) Bi-shart al-wasf is absolutely more general than mada al-wasf
C) They are equivalent in all cases
D) They are related by 'umum min wajh (partial overlap)

Key Takeaway
The two interpretations of the general conditional are related by partial overlap, not absolute generality, and which one applies depends on whether the description is necessary for the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition: 'Every intoxicating drink is prohibited as long as it is intoxicating.' Determine whether this is an instance of bi-shart al-wasf or mada al-wasf, and explain why, using the criteria from the passage (whether the description is necessary for the subject at that time).
