Two Meanings of the Conditioned Proposition
Distinguishing two meanings of mashruta

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two meanings of the conditioned proposition (المشروطة) by analyzing whether the description is a container for necessity or part of the subject.

Core Idea
In 'as long as', necessity attaches to the essence; in 'under the condition', it attaches to the composite.

The conditioned proposition (المشروطة) is a key modal operator in Islamic logic. It comes in two forms: one that considers the description as merely a temporal frame ('as long as'), and one that includes the description as part of the subject for necessity ('under the condition'). This lesson unpacks this distinction through a concrete example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example
Take the proposition 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer.' This sentence is ambiguous between two meanings.
2
Apply the first meaning (مادام)
Interpret 'as long as he is a writer' as specifying the time during which the necessity holds. The description 'writer' is a container, not part of the subject. Under this reading, the proposition is true: the necessity holds while writing.
3
Apply the second meaning (بشرط)
Interpret 'under the condition of being a writer' as making the necessity conditional on the subject being a writer, i.e., the necessity attaches to the composite 'writer that writes'. Here, the description is part of the subject. Under this reading, the proposition is false because finger movement is not necessary for the writer's essence as such.
4
Understand the key difference
In the first, the description is a container (ظرف), so necessity relates to the essence. In the second, the description is part (جزء) of that to which necessity is attributed. If we treat it as both, we get redundancy.
The author explains that considering the description twice would be useless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنا إذا قلنا كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع بالضرورة مادام كاتباً» وأردنا المعنى الأول صدقت كما تبيّن، وإن أردنا المعنى الثاني كذبت، لأن حركة الأصابع ليست ضرورية الثبوت لذات الكاتب في شيء من الأوقات، فإنّ الوصف كان الوصف هناك معتبراً على أنه ظرف للضرورة، لاجزء لمانسب إليه الضرورة، وإلا لزم اعتبار الوصف مرتين : مرة جزء لما نسب إليه الضرورة، ومرة ظرفاً للضرورة، فيصير المعنى أن نسبة المحمول ضرورية لمجموع ذات الموضوع مع وصفه في جميع أوقات وصفه، ولا فائدة لاعتبار الظرف هاهنا. فتعين أنه إذا اعتبرت مادام الوصف كان ضرورة نسبة المحمول إلى ذات الموضوع فقط، وحينئذ إن لم يكن الوصف الذي له مدخل في تحقق الضرورة ضرورياً لذات الموضوع حال ثبوته له - كالكتابة – صدقت المشروطة بشرط الوصف - دون مادام الوصف - وإن كان ضرورياً له في زمان ثبوتـه لـه صدقت المشروطة بالمعنيين معاً كقولك : كلّ منخسف فهو مظلم مادام منخسفاً، سواء أريد منه بشرط كونه منخسفاً أو مادام منخسفاً بلا اعتبار الاشتراط، بناء على أن الانحساف ضروري للقمر في وقت معين – وهو وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس - فإن نسبة الإظلام إلى مجموع القمر ووصف الانحساف كان ضرورياً له، وإن نسبته إلى ذات القمر، كان أيضاً ضرورياً له في وقت الانحساف ؛ لأن القمر في ذلك الوقت يستحيل وجوده بلا انحساف على - مازعموا - فذات القمر مستلزم للمجموع من ذاته ووصف الإنخساف، وهذا المجموع مستلزم للإظلام، ومستلزم المستلزم مستلزم، فذات القمر في ذلك الوقت مستلزم للإظلام ؛ فظهر بذلك أن النسبة بين معنيي المشروطة هي العموم من وجه. وهذا الكلام ، محقق، وقد أخطأ فيه كثيرون، وزعموا أن النسبة بينهما العموم مطلقاً، لأن مادام الوصف أعم مطلقاً (شريف).
الكتابة التي هي شرط تحقق الضرورة غير ضرورية لذات الكاتب في زمان أصلاً، فما ظنك بالمشروطة بها.
فالمشروطة العامة بالمعنى الأوّل أعم من الضرورية والدائمة من وجه لأنك قد سمعت أن ذات الموضوع قد تكون عين وصفه وقد تكون غيره، فإذا أتحدا وكانت المادة مادة الضرورة صدقت القضايا الثلاث كقولنا : كلّ إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، أو دائماً ، أو مادام إنساناً، وإن تغايرا فإن كانت المادة مادة الضرورة ولم يكن للوصف دخل في تحقق الضرورة صدقت الضرورية والدائمة دون المشروطة، كقولنا : «كل كاتب حيوان بالضرورة، أو دائماً لا بالضرورة مادام كاتباً»، فإن وصف الكتابة لادخل له في ضرورة ثبوت الحيوان لذات الكاتب، وإن لم تكن المادة مادة الضرورة الذاتية والدوام الذاتي وكان هناك ضرورة بشرط الوصف صدقت المشروطة دون الضرورية والدائمة كما في المثال المذكور، فإنّ تحرك الأصابع ليس بضروري ولادائم لذات الكاتب بل بشرط الكتابة.
وأما المشروطة بالمعنى الثاني فهي أعم من الضرورية مطلقاً، لأنه متى ثبتت الضرورة في جميع أوقات الذات ثبتت في جميع أوقات الوصف بدون العكس.
ومن الدائمة من وجه، لتصادقهما في مادة الضرورة المطلقة، وصدق الدائمة بدونها حيث يخلو الدوام عن الضرورة وبالعكس حيث تكون الضرورة والدوام في جميع أوقات الوصف، ولا تدوم في جميع أوقات الذات.
If we say 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer' and we intend the first meaning, it is true as shown, and if we intend the second meaning, it is false, because the movement of fingers is not a necessary attribute of the essence of the writer at any time. For the description there is considered as a container for the necessity, not a part of that to which necessity is attributed; otherwise, the description would be considered twice: once as part of that to which necessity is attributed, and once as a container for the necessity, so the meaning would become that the attribution of the predicate is necessary to the combination of the subject's essence and its description in all times of the description, and there is no benefit to considering the container here. So it is determined that if 'as long as the description' is considered, it is the necessity of the predicate's relation to the subject's essence only. Then if the description that has a role in realizing the necessity is not necessary for the subject's essence at the time it is attributed to it — like writing — the conditioned proposition with the condition of the description is true, but not the 'as long as the description' one. And if it is necessary for it at the time of its attribution to it, then the conditioned proposition with both meanings is true, like your saying: 'Every eclipsed thing is dark as long as it is eclipsed,' whether it is intended with the condition of being eclipsed or as long as it is eclipsed without considering the condition, based on the fact that eclipse is necessary for the moon at a specific time — namely the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun. For the relation of darkness to the combination of the moon and the attribute of eclipse was necessary for it, and its relation to the essence of the moon was also necessary for it at the time of eclipse, because the moon at that time is impossible to exist without eclipse according to what they claim. So the essence of the moon entails the combination of itself and the attribute of eclipse, and this combination entails darkness, and the entailer of the entailer is an entailer, so the essence of the moon at that time entails darkness. Thus it appears that the relationship between the two meanings of the conditioned proposition is generality in some respects (عموم من وجه). And this statement is verified, but many have erred, claiming that the relationship between them is absolute generality, because 'as long as the description' is absolutely more general (Sharif).
Writing, which is the condition for realizing necessity, is not necessary for the essence of the writer at any time, so what do you think of the conditioned proposition with it?
Thus the general conditioned proposition in the first sense is more general than the necessary and perpetual in some respects, because you have heard that the subject's essence may be identical to its description or may be other than it. If they are identical and the matter is a matter of necessity, the three propositions are true, like our saying: 'Every human is an animal necessarily, or perpetually, or as long as he is human.' If they differ, then if the matter is a matter of necessity and the description has no role in realizing necessity, the necessary and perpetual are true without the conditioned, like our saying: 'Every writer is an animal necessarily or perpetually, not necessarily as long as he is a writer,' for the description of writing has no role in the necessity of animal's attribution to the writer's essence. And if the matter is not a matter of essential necessity and essential perpetuity, and there is a necessity under the condition of the description, the conditioned proposition is true without the necessary and perpetual, as in the mentioned example, for movement of fingers is neither necessary nor perpetual for the writer's essence, but rather under the condition of writing.
As for the conditioned proposition in the second sense, it is absolutely more general than the necessary, because whenever necessity is established in all times of the essence, it is established in all times of the description, but not vice versa. And it is more general than the perpetual in some respects, because they coincide in the matter of absolute necessity, and the perpetual is true without it where perpetuity is devoid of necessity, and vice versa where necessity and perpetuity occur in all times of the description but do not persist in all times of the essence.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definitional and comparative analysis of the conditioned proposition (المشروطة) in modal logic. The author (Qutbi) distinguishes two meanings of the conditioned proposition: one with 'as long as the description' (مادام الوصف) and one with 'under the condition of the description' (بشرط الوصف). The passage begins with an example: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer.' If we intend the first meaning (مادام), the proposition is true; if we intend the second (بشرط), it is false, because the movement of fingers is not necessary for the writer's essence at any time; it is only necessary while writing. The author explains that when we say 'as long as the description', the description is only a container or time-frame for the necessity, not a part of the subject to which necessity is attributed. If we treated it as a part, we would need to consider the description twice, which is redundant. Therefore, in 'as long as the description', the necessity relates only to the subject's essence, not to the combination. Then he discusses cases where the description itself is necessary for the subject at that time, like the moon's eclipse. In such cases, both meanings are true. The relationship between the two meanings is 'generality in some respects' (عموم من وجه), meaning they partially overlap. Many scholars mistakenly thought 'as long as' is absolutely more general, but the author corrects this. After that, the passage compares the first meaning of the conditioned proposition with the necessary (الضرورية) and perpetual (الدائمة) propositions. When the subject's essence is identical to its description (e.g., human is animal), all three are true when the matter is necessary. When they differ and the description is not essential (e.g., writer is animal), the necessary and perpetual are true but the conditioned is false. When the matter is not essential but there is necessity under condition (e.g., writer moves fingers), only the conditioned is true. Thus, the first meaning is more general than necessary and perpetual in some respects. The second meaning is absolutely more general than necessary, because necessity in all times of essence implies necessity in all times of description, but not conversely. It is more general than perpetual in some respects, as they coincide in absolute necessity but differ elsewhere.

Key Terms
المشروطة
مادام الوصف
بشرط الوصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The interpretation with بشرط الوصف

D) Both are true

Key Takeaway
The conditioned proposition has two distinct meanings based on whether the description is a container or a part of the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Relation Between the Two Meanings: Generality in Some Respects
Establishing partial overlap relation between the two meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the logical relationship between the two meanings of mashruta as عموم من وجه (partial overlap) and refute the incorrect claim that مادام is absolutely more general.

Core Idea
The two meanings overlap partially, but neither is absolutely more general.

The author clarifies that many scholars mistakenly thought 'as long as the description' is absolutely more general than 'under the condition'. Using the example of the lunar eclipse, he shows that both can be true together, establishing a relationship of generality in some respects (عموم من وجه).

Guided Path
1
Examine when both meanings are true
Consider 'Every eclipsed moon is dark as long as it is eclipsed.' According to the author, this is true under both interpretations because the eclipse is necessary for the moon at that time, making the necessity attach both to the essence and the composite.
2
Examine when one is true and the other false
Recall the writer example: the first meaning (مادام) is true, the second (بشرط) is false. So there is an instance where the first is true and the second false.
3
Derive the relationship
From steps 1 and 2, we see that: (a) sometimes both are true; (b) sometimes the first is true and the second false. The author says the relationship is 'generality in some respects' (عموم من وجه), meaning they partially overlap but each may have instances not covered by the other. Thus, the claim that مادام is absolutely more general is false.
4
Contrast with the mistaken view
Many scholars claimed that مادام is absolutely more general, but the author shows that this view is incorrect. The relationship is not one of absolute inclusion but partial overlap.
This analysis relies on the examples given; further cases could be explored.

Source Text

الكتابة التي هي شرط تحقق الضرورة غير ضرورية لذات الكاتب في زمان أصلاً، فما ظنك بالمشروطة بها.

وأما المشروطة بالمعنى الثاني فهي أعم من الضرورية مطلقاً، لأنه متى ثبتت الضرورة في جميع أوقات الذات ثبتت في جميع أوقات الوصف بدون العكس.
ومن الدائمة من وجه، لتصادقهما في مادة الضرورة المطلقة، وصدق الدائمة بدونها حيث يخلو الدوام عن الضرورة وبالعكس حيث تكون الضرورة والدوام في جميع أوقات الوصف، ولا تدوم في جميع أوقات الذات.
If we say 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer' and we intend the first meaning, it is true as shown, and if we intend the second meaning, it is false, because the movement of fingers is not a necessary attribute of the essence of the writer at any time. For the description there is considered as a container for the necessity, not a part of that to which necessity is attributed; otherwise, the description would be considered twice: once as part of that to which necessity is attributed, and once as a container for the necessity, so the meaning would become that the attribution of the predicate is necessary to the combination of the subject's essence and its description in all times of the description, and there is no benefit to considering the container here. So it is determined that if 'as long as the description' is considered, it is the necessity of the predicate's relation to the subject's essence only. Then if the description that has a role in realizing the necessity is not necessary for the subject's essence at the time it is attributed to it — like writing — the conditioned proposition with the condition of the description is true, but not the 'as long as the description' one. And if it is necessary for it at the time of its attribution to it, then the conditioned proposition with both meanings is true, like your saying: 'Every eclipsed thing is dark as long as it is eclipsed,' whether it is intended with the condition of being eclipsed or as long as it is eclipsed without considering the condition, based on the fact that eclipse is necessary for the moon at a specific time — namely the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun. For the relation of darkness to the combination of the moon and the attribute of eclipse was necessary for it, and its relation to the essence of the moon was also necessary for it at the time of eclipse, because the moon at that time is impossible to exist without eclipse according to what they claim. So the essence of the moon entails the combination of itself and the attribute of eclipse, and this combination entails darkness, and the entailer of the entailer is an entailer, so the essence of the moon at that time entails darkness. Thus it appears that the relationship between the two meanings of the conditioned proposition is generality in some respects (عموم من وجه). And this statement is verified, but many have erred, claiming that the relationship between them is absolute generality, because 'as long as the description' is absolutely more general (Sharif).
Writing, which is the condition for realizing necessity, is not necessary for the essence of the writer at any time, so what do you think of the conditioned proposition with it?
Thus the general conditioned proposition in the first sense is more general than the necessary and perpetual in some respects, because you have heard that the subject's essence may be identical to its description or may be other than it. If they are identical and the matter is a matter of necessity, the three propositions are true, like our saying: 'Every human is an animal necessarily, or perpetually, or as long as he is human.' If they differ, then if the matter is a matter of necessity and the description has no role in realizing necessity, the necessary and perpetual are true without the conditioned, like our saying: 'Every writer is an animal necessarily or perpetually, not necessarily as long as he is a writer,' for the description of writing has no role in the necessity of animal's attribution to the writer's essence. And if the matter is not a matter of essential necessity and essential perpetuity, and there is a necessity under the condition of the description, the conditioned proposition is true without the necessary and perpetual, as in the mentioned example, for movement of fingers is neither necessary nor perpetual for the writer's essence, but rather under the condition of writing.
As for the conditioned proposition in the second sense, it is absolutely more general than the necessary, because whenever necessity is established in all times of the essence, it is established in all times of the description, but not vice versa. And it is more general than the perpetual in some respects, because they coincide in the matter of absolute necessity, and the perpetual is true without it where perpetuity is devoid of necessity, and vice versa where necessity and perpetuity occur in all times of the description but do not persist in all times of the essence.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definitional and comparative analysis of the conditioned proposition (المشروطة) in modal logic. The author (Qutbi) distinguishes two meanings of the conditioned proposition: one with 'as long as the description' (مادام الوصف) and one with 'under the condition of the description' (بشرط الوصف). The passage begins with an example: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer.' If we intend the first meaning (مادام), the proposition is true; if we intend the second (بشرط), it is false, because the movement of fingers is not necessary for the writer's essence at any time; it is only necessary while writing. The author explains that when we say 'as long as the description', the description is only a container or time-frame for the necessity, not a part of the subject to which necessity is attributed. If we treated it as a part, we would need to consider the description twice, which is redundant. Therefore, in 'as long as the description', the necessity relates only to the subject's essence, not to the combination. Then he discusses cases where the description itself is necessary for the subject at that time, like the moon's eclipse. In such cases, both meanings are true. The relationship between the two meanings is 'generality in some respects' (عموم من وجه), meaning they partially overlap. Many scholars mistakenly thought 'as long as' is absolutely more general, but the author corrects this. After that, the passage compares the first meaning of the conditioned proposition with the necessary (الضرورية) and perpetual (الدائمة) propositions. When the subject's essence is identical to its description (e.g., human is animal), all three are true when the matter is necessary. When they differ and the description is not essential (e.g., writer is animal), the necessary and perpetual are true but the conditioned is false. When the matter is not essential but there is necessity under condition (e.g., writer moves fingers), only the conditioned is true. Thus, the first meaning is more general than necessary and perpetual in some respects. The second meaning is absolutely more general than necessary, because necessity in all times of essence implies necessity in all times of description, but not conversely. It is more general than perpetual in some respects, as they coincide in absolute necessity but differ elsewhere.

بشرط الوصف

intermediate

Comparing First Meaning of Mashruta with Necessary and Perpetual
Comparing first meaning of mashruta with dharuriyya and da'ima

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the truth conditions of the conditioned proposition (first meaning) with the necessary (الضرورية) and perpetual (الدائمة) propositions in various subject-description scenarios.

Core Idea
The first meaning of mashruta is more general than dharuriyya and da'ima in some respects but not absolutely.

The author examines how the conditioned proposition (in its first meaning, مادام) relates to two other common modal propositions: the necessary (where the predicate holds for the subject in all times of its essence) and the perpetual (where it holds at all times, not necessarily essentially). Through examples like human/animal, writer/animal, and writer/fingers, he shows when each type is true.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three proposition types
The necessary (الضرورية): predicate holds for the subject in all times of its essence, e.g., 'Every human is an animal necessarily.' The perpetual (الدائمة): predicate holds at all times, but not essentially, e.g., 'Every human is an animal perpetually' (same example because it is essential). The conditioned (المشروطة first meaning): predicate holds for the subject's essence as long as a description holds, e.g., 'Every human is an animal as long as he is human.'
2
Case: subject and description are identical
When the subject's essence is the same as its description (e.g., human = human), and the matter is necessary (animal for human), all three propositions are true. Example: 'Every human is an animal' is necessary, perpetual, and true as long as a human.
3
Case: subject and description differ, description not essential
Example: 'Every writer is an animal.' Writing is not essential; but being animal is necessary for the essence. So the necessary and perpetual are true (animal is always true of writer), but the conditioned (as long as writer) is false because animality is not tied to the writing description.
4
Case: necessity is conditional on description
Example: 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer.' Here, necessary and perpetual are false (not always true), but the conditioned is true because the necessity is tied to the writing description. Thus, the conditioned can be true when the others are false.
5
Conclude the relationship
From these cases, the first meaning of mashruta is more general than necessary and perpetual in the sense that it can be true when they are false (case 3), and they can be true when it is false (case 2). They agree in case 1. So the relationship is again 'generality in some respects'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنا إذا قلنا كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع بالضرورة مادام كاتباً» وأردنا المعنى الأول صدقت كما تبيّن، وإن أردنا المعنى الثاني كذبت، لأن حركة الأصابع ليست ضرورية الثبوت لذات الكاتب في شيء من الأوقات، فإنّ الوصف كان الوصف هناك معتبراً على أنه ظرف للضرورة، لاجزء لمانسب إليه الضرورة، وإلا لزم اعتبار الوصف مرتين : مرة جزء لما نسب إليه الضرورة، ومرة ظرفاً للضرورة، فيصير المعنى أن نسبة المحمول ضرورية لمجموع ذات الموضوع مع وصفه في جميع أوقات وصفه، ولا فائدة لاعتبار الظرف هاهنا. فتعين أنه إذا اعتبرت مادام الوصف كان ضرورة نسبة المحمول إلى ذات الموضوع فقط، وحينئذ إن لم يكن الوصف الذي له مدخل في تحقق الضرورة ضرورياً لذات الموضوع حال ثبوته له - كالكتابة – صدقت المشروطة بشرط الوصف - دون مادام الوصف - وإن كان ضرورياً له في زمان ثبوتـه لـه صدقت المشروطة بالمعنيين معاً كقولك : كلّ منخسف فهو مظلم مادام منخسفاً، سواء أريد منه بشرط كونه منخسفاً أو مادام منخسفاً بلا اعتبار الاشتراط، بناء على أن الانحساف ضروري للقمر في وقت معين – وهو وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس - فإن نسبة الإظلام إلى مجموع القمر ووصف الانحساف كان ضرورياً له، وإن نسبته إلى ذات القمر، كان أيضاً ضرورياً له في وقت الانحساف ؛ لأن القمر في ذلك الوقت يستحيل وجوده بلا انحساف على - مازعموا - فذات القمر مستلزم للمجموع من ذاته ووصف الإنخساف، وهذا المجموع مستلزم للإظلام، ومستلزم المستلزم مستلزم، فذات القمر في ذلك الوقت مستلزم للإظلام ؛ فظهر بذلك أن النسبة بين معنيي المشروطة هي العموم من وجه. وهذا الكلام ، محقق، وقد أخطأ فيه كثيرون، وزعموا أن النسبة بينهما العموم مطلقاً، لأن مادام الوصف أعم مطلقاً (شريف).
الكتابة التي هي شرط تحقق الضرورة غير ضرورية لذات الكاتب في زمان أصلاً، فما ظنك بالمشروطة بها.
فالمشروطة العامة بالمعنى الأوّل أعم من الضرورية والدائمة من وجه لأنك قد سمعت أن ذات الموضوع قد تكون عين وصفه وقد تكون غيره، فإذا أتحدا وكانت المادة مادة الضرورة صدقت القضايا الثلاث كقولنا : كلّ إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، أو دائماً ، أو مادام إنساناً، وإن تغايرا فإن كانت المادة مادة الضرورة ولم يكن للوصف دخل في تحقق الضرورة صدقت الضرورية والدائمة دون المشروطة، كقولنا : «كل كاتب حيوان بالضرورة، أو دائماً لا بالضرورة مادام كاتباً»، فإن وصف الكتابة لادخل له في ضرورة ثبوت الحيوان لذات الكاتب، وإن لم تكن المادة مادة الضرورة الذاتية والدوام الذاتي وكان هناك ضرورة بشرط الوصف صدقت المشروطة دون الضرورية والدائمة كما في المثال المذكور، فإنّ تحرك الأصابع ليس بضروري ولادائم لذات الكاتب بل بشرط الكتابة.
وأما المشروطة بالمعنى الثاني فهي أعم من الضرورية مطلقاً، لأنه متى ثبتت الضرورة في جميع أوقات الذات ثبتت في جميع أوقات الوصف بدون العكس.
ومن الدائمة من وجه، لتصادقهما في مادة الضرورة المطلقة، وصدق الدائمة بدونها حيث يخلو الدوام عن الضرورة وبالعكس حيث تكون الضرورة والدوام في جميع أوقات الوصف، ولا تدوم في جميع أوقات الذات.
If we say 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer' and we intend the first meaning, it is true as shown, and if we intend the second meaning, it is false, because the movement of fingers is not a necessary attribute of the essence of the writer at any time. For the description there is considered as a container for the necessity, not a part of that to which necessity is attributed; otherwise, the description would be considered twice: once as part of that to which necessity is attributed, and once as a container for the necessity, so the meaning would become that the attribution of the predicate is necessary to the combination of the subject's essence and its description in all times of the description, and there is no benefit to considering the container here. So it is determined that if 'as long as the description' is considered, it is the necessity of the predicate's relation to the subject's essence only. Then if the description that has a role in realizing the necessity is not necessary for the subject's essence at the time it is attributed to it — like writing — the conditioned proposition with the condition of the description is true, but not the 'as long as the description' one. And if it is necessary for it at the time of its attribution to it, then the conditioned proposition with both meanings is true, like your saying: 'Every eclipsed thing is dark as long as it is eclipsed,' whether it is intended with the condition of being eclipsed or as long as it is eclipsed without considering the condition, based on the fact that eclipse is necessary for the moon at a specific time — namely the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun. For the relation of darkness to the combination of the moon and the attribute of eclipse was necessary for it, and its relation to the essence of the moon was also necessary for it at the time of eclipse, because the moon at that time is impossible to exist without eclipse according to what they claim. So the essence of the moon entails the combination of itself and the attribute of eclipse, and this combination entails darkness, and the entailer of the entailer is an entailer, so the essence of the moon at that time entails darkness. Thus it appears that the relationship between the two meanings of the conditioned proposition is generality in some respects (عموم من وجه). And this statement is verified, but many have erred, claiming that the relationship between them is absolute generality, because 'as long as the description' is absolutely more general (Sharif).

The General Conventional Modal (al-'Urfiyya al-'Amma)
Author defines the fourth modal proposition and explains its naming and relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the general 'urfiyya proposition and explain its logical relationships to the general mushruta, necessary, and perpetual propositions.

Core Idea
The general 'urfiyya judges permanence under a description, and custom is its source.

This lesson introduces the fourth modal proposition in the classification of modal propositions. The author defines it, gives examples, explains its naming from custom (al-'urf), and clarifies its logical hierarchy with respect to other modals like the general mushruta and the necessary/perpetual. Understanding this modal is crucial for precise formulation of conditional statements in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of the general 'urfiyya
Locate the sentence that defines this modal: it is a proposition where judgment of permanence (dawām) of the predicate holds as long as (mā dāma) the subject's essence is described by a title (al-'unwān).
The key phrase is 'مادام ذات الموضوع متصفاً بالعنوان'.
2
Analyze the examples given
The author repeats the examples from the general mushruta: 'Always every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer' and its negative. Note that the condition 'as long as he is a writer' restricts the judgment to the duration of the description.
These examples are the same as for mushruta, but the modality differs: here it is permanence under description, not necessity.
3
Understand the naming: 'urfiyya and 'amma
It is called 'urfiyya because common usage ('urf) understands this meaning from an unqualified negative statement (e.g., 'No sleeper is awake' is understood as 'as long as he sleeps'). It is 'general' because it is more comprehensive than the particular 'urfiyya (a compound proposition).
The naming shows how Islamic logicians derive technical terms from everyday language.
4
Map the logical relationships to other modals
The general 'urfiyya is absolutely more general than the general mushruta, necessary, and perpetual: if any of those hold, the general 'urfiyya holds, but not vice versa. This is because necessity or permanence in all times of the essence implies permanence under the description, but the converse is false.
This hierarchy is important for converting between modal propositions in arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة: العرفية العامة، وهي التي حكم فيها بدوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه مادام ذات الموضوع متصفاً بالعنوان. ومثالها إيجاباً وسلباً ما مر في المشروطة العامة من قولنا: «دائماً كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع مادام كاتباً» و «دائماً لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع مادام كاتباً». وإنما سميت «عرفية» لأن العرف إنما يفهم هذا المعنى من السالبة إذا أطلقت، حتى إذا قيل: «لا شيء من النائم بمستيقظ» يفهم العرف المستيقظ مسلوب عن النائم مادام نائماً، فلما أخذ هذا المعنى من العرف نسبت إليه. و «عامة» لأنها أعم من العرفية الخاصة التي هي من المركبات. وهي أعم مطلقاً من المشروطة العامة، فإنه متى تحققت الضرورة بحسب الوصف تحقق الدوام بحسب الوصف من غير عكس، وكذا من الضرورية والدائمة، لأنه متى صدقت الضرورة أو الدوام في جميع أوقات الذات صدق الدوام في جميع أوقات الوصف ولا ينعكس.
The fourth: the general 'urfiyya, which is that in which judgment is made of the permanence of the predicate for the subject or its negation from it as long as the essence of the subject is described by the title. Its example, positively and negatively, is what passed in the general mushruta from our saying: 'Always every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer' and 'Always nothing of the writer is still-fingered as long as he is a writer.' It is called 'urfiyya because custom (al-'urf) understands this meaning from the negative when it is unqualified, such that when it is said: 'Nothing of the sleeper is awake' custom understands that 'awake' is negated from the sleeper as long as he is sleeping. So when this meaning was taken from custom, it was attributed to it. And 'general' because it is more general than the particular 'urfiyya which is one of the compound propositions. It is absolutely more general than the general mushruta, for whenever necessity is realized according to the description, permanence is realized according to the description without the reverse, and similarly [more general] than the necessary and the perpetual, because whenever the necessary or the perpetual is true in all times of the essence, the perpetual is true in all times of the description, and not vice versa.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth modal proposition in the classification of modal propositions (al-qadaya al-muwajjaha). The author states that al-'urfiyya al-'amma is a proposition where the judgment of the predicate (positive or negative) holds continuously as long as the subject's essence is described by the given title (al-'unwan). The examples given are the same as those for the general mushruta: 'Always every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer' and its negative counterpart. The naming is explained: it is called 'urfiyya because common usage ('urf) understands this meaning from an unqualified negative statement. For instance, if one says 'No sleeper is awake', custom understands it as meaning 'as long as he is sleeping'. Thus the term is taken from custom. It is called 'amma (general) because it is more comprehensive than the particular 'urfiyya, which is one of the compound propositions (i.e., requiring a specific relation). The author then clarifies its logical relationships: the general 'urfiyya is absolutely more general than the general mushruta: if necessity holds with respect to the description, then permanence holds with respect to the description, but not vice versa. Similarly, it is more general than the necessary and the perpetual propositions: if the necessary or perpetual holds in all times of the essence, then the perpetual holds in all times of the description, but the converse does not hold. This shows the hierarchical order among these modals.

Key Terms
عرفية عامة
دوام
عنوان
مشروطة عامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a general 'urfiyya proposition?
A) Every human is an animal necessarily.
B) Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer.
C) No sleeper is awake at any time.
D) The sun is rising always.

Key Takeaway
The general 'urfiyya proposition expresses permanence of the predicate as long as the subject is described by a specific title, making it more general than necessity and perpetual, and its name derives from custom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Propositions

The General Absolute: Definition and Uniqueness
Defines the fifth modal and explains its single meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the general absolute proposition and explain why it has only one meaning rather than two.

Core Idea
The general absolute simply affirms the predicate; its permanence for the composite guarantees permanence for the essence.

In the previous lesson, we saw that al-mushruta had two meanings due to different condition interpretations. The fifth modal, al-mutlaqa al-'amma, avoids this complexity entirely. Here the author defines it and gives the logical reason why no second meaning is needed. Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to classify propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrase 'general absolute'
Note that 'general absolute' means the predicate is affirmed of the subject without any temporal or conditional qualification. It is the simplest modal form.
Contrast with the conditioned modals (e.g., mushruta) that include a condition.
2
Understand the footnote's logical argument
The footnote argues: if the predicate is permanent (dawam) for the combination of essence and description, then it is permanent for the essence alone during the time of the description. This is because permanence means continuity and non-separation. Hence, no second interpretation is needed.
The two meanings of mushruta arose because the condition could be attached to the subject or the predicate; here the permanence is unconditional.
3
Analyze the two examples given
The first example is 'Every writer moves his fingers at the time of writing' (where the description causes permanence). The second is 'Every writer is an animal' (where the predicate is permanent regardless of the description). Both fall under the general absolute because the predicate is affirmed of the subject without condition.
In the second example, 'animal' is always true of the writer's essence, even when not writing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة المطلقة العامة، وهي التي حكم فيها بثبوت المحمول للموضوع (١) لم يعتبر هاهنا معنيان على قياس معنيي المشروطة، لأن المحمول إذا كان دائماً للمجموع الذات والوصف كان دائماً للذات في زمان الوصف، لأن معنى الدوام استمراره وعدم انفكاكه وهو حاصل بالقياس إلى المجموع وبالقياس إلى الذات وحده في زمان الوصف، سواء كان للوصف مدخل في دوام المحمول -كما مر في المثال المذكور- أولم يكن كما في قولك : «كل كاتب حيوان» (شريف)
The fifth: the general absolute, which is that in which judgment is made of the affirmation of the predicate for the subject. (1) Two meanings are not considered here on the model of the two meanings of the mushruta, because if the predicate is permanent for the combination of the essence and the description, it is permanent for the essence in the time of the description, because the meaning of permanence is its continuity and non-separation, and it is obtained with respect to the combination and with respect to the essence alone in the time of the description, whether the description has an input in the permanence of the predicate—as passed in the mentioned example—or not, as in your saying: 'Every writer is an animal' (Sharif).
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth and final modal proposition in the series: al-mutlaqa al-'amma (the general absolute). The author states that it is a proposition in which the predicate is affirmed of the subject without any qualification of time or condition. The attached footnote explains why this modal does not have two meanings like the mushruta (which had two interpretations: necessity by condition of the description, or necessity by itself). The reason given is that if the predicate is permanent for the combination of the essence and the description (i.e., the composite of subject and its description), then it is necessarily permanent for the essence alone during the time of the description. The meaning of permanence (dawam) is continuity and non-separation. This holds regardless of whether the description plays a role in the permanence of the predicate (as in the earlier example of 'writer moving fingers') or not (as in the example 'Every writer is an animal', where 'animal' is permanent for the writer's essence irrespective of the description 'writer'). Thus, no ambiguity arises. The footnote ends with '(Sharif)', likely a marginal note indicating the commentator.

Key Terms
مطلقة عامة
دوام
مجموع الذات والوصف

Key Takeaway
The general absolute avoids ambiguity because the permanence of the predicate for the combined essence-and-description automatically guarantees its permanence for the essence alone in that time.

The General Absolute Proposition (al-Mutlaqa al-'Amma)
Defines the general absolute proposition and its relation to other propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a general absolute proposition and explain why it is more general than necessary and perpetual propositions.

Core Idea
The general absolute affirms actuality without modal commitment, making it the broadest proposition type.

In this lesson, we examine the fifth type of proposition: the general absolute (al-mutlaqa al-'amma). This proposition asserts that the predicate actually holds for the subject, without any qualification of permanence or necessity. Understanding this concept is crucial because it serves as the most inclusive category—every modal proposition implies actuality, but actuality does not imply any specific modality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Locate the term being defined: 'al-mutlaqa al-'amma' (the general absolute). Note that it is the fifth type of proposition discussed in the text.
2
Extract the definition
The definition states that it is a judgment of affirming or negating the predicate for the subject 'actually' (bi al-fi'l). This means the relation holds in reality, but without any modal condition such as permanence (dawam) or necessity (darura).
3
Understand the naming
It is called 'absolute' (mutlaqa) because when a proposition is left unrestricted (without modal qualifiers), what is understood is the actuality of the relation. It is called 'general' (amma) because it is more general than the existential propositions that are neither perpetual nor necessary, and also more general than the four preceding modal propositions.
4
Analyze the examples
Two examples are given: affirmative ('Every human is breathing with general unrestrictedness') and negative ('Nothing of the human is breathing with general unrestrictedness'). Observe that these propositions assert actual occurrences, not necessary or perpetual ones.
The phrase 'with general unrestrictedness' (bi al-itlaq al-'amm) emphasizes the lack of modal restriction.
5
Compare with modal propositions
The text explains that whenever a necessary or perpetual proposition is true, the relation is actual, but actuality does not entail necessity or perpetuity. Therefore, the general absolute is more general—it includes all actual relations, whether modal or not, while modal propositions are a subset.
This asymmetry is key: actuality is a necessary condition for necessity, but not sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة المطلقة العامة، وهي التي حكم فيها بثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه بالفعل؛ أما الإيجاب فكقولنا : «كل إنسان متنفس بالإطلاق العام». وأمّا السلب فكقولنا : لاشيء من الإنسان بمتنفس بالإطلاق العام». وإنما كانت مطلقة لأن القضية إذا أطلقت ولم تقيد بقيد – من دوام أو ضرورة أو لادوام أو لا ضرورة - يفهم منها فعليّة النسبة، فلما كان هذا المعنى مفهوم القضية المطلقة تسمّى بها، وإنما كانت عامة لأنها أعم من الوجودية اللادائمة واللاضرورية كما سيجيء. وهي أعم من القضايا الأربع المتقدّمة، لأنه متى صدقت ضرورة أو دوام بحسب الذات أو بحسب الوصف تكون النسبة فعلية، وليس يلزم من فعلية النسبة ضرورتها أو دوامها.
The fifth: the general absolute, which is that in which judgment is made of the affirmation of the predicate for the subject or its negation from it actually. As for affirmation, it is like our saying: 'Every human is breathing with general unrestrictedness.' As for negation, it is like our saying: 'Nothing of the human is breathing with general unrestrictedness.' It was called 'absolute' because when the proposition is unrestricted and not qualified by any restriction – of permanence, necessity, non-permanence, or non-necessity – actuality of the relation is understood from it. So when this meaning was the concept of the absolute proposition, it was named by it. And it was called 'general' because it is more general than the non-perpetual and non-necessary existential, as will come. And it is more general than the four preceding propositions, because whenever necessity or perpetuity according to the essence or according to the description is true, the relation is actual, but actuality of the relation does not entail its necessity or perpetuity.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage defines the 'general absolute' (al-mutlaqa al-'amma) proposition, the fifth type. It asserts the actual affirmation or negation of the predicate for the subject, without any modal qualification. Examples: 'Every human is breathing with general unrestrictedness' (affirmative) and 'Nothing of the human is breathing with general unrestrictedness' (negative). The term 'absolute' (mutlaqa) is used because when a proposition is stated without any modal qualifier (such as permanence, necessity, non-permanence, or non-necessity), what is understood is the actual occurrence of the relation (fi'liyya al-nisba). Thus, the proposition is named after this meaning. It is called 'general' (amma) because it is more general than the existential propositions that are neither perpetual nor necessary, as will be explained later. Furthermore, it is more general than the four preceding types of modal propositions (necessary, perpetual, conditional, customary). This is because if a necessary or perpetual proposition holds (whether according to the subject's essence or according to a description), then the relation is actual; but actuality of the relation does not imply its necessity or perpetuity. The key point is that the general absolute has the widest extension among the five types discussed so far.

Key Terms
المطلقة العامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because it applies to all subjects.

Key Takeaway
The general absolute proposition asserts actuality without any modal qualification, making it the most fundamental and inclusive proposition type in modal logic.

The General Possible Proposition (al-Mumkina al-'Amma)
Defines the general possible proposition and explains its interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the general possible proposition and interpret its meaning for both affirmative and negative judgments.

Core Idea
The general possible negates the necessity of the opposite, not the truth of the judgment.

In this lesson, we examine the sixth type of proposition: the general possible. This modal proposition does not assert that something actually exists or does not exist; rather, it asserts that the opposite is not necessary. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating modal arguments in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the judgment's quality
Determine whether the proposition's judgment is affirmative or negative. For example, 'Every fire is hot' is affirmative; 'Nothing of the hot is cold' is negative.
The quality determines which side is the 'opposite'.
2
Find the opposite side
For an affirmative judgment, the opposite is the negation of the predicate (e.g., 'fire is not hot'). For a negative judgment, the opposite is the affirmation (e.g., 'some hot is cold').
The opposite is always the contradictory of the judgment.
3
Apply the definition
The general possible negates absolute necessity from that opposite side. So 'Every fire is hot with general possibility' means it is not necessary that fire is not hot.
The proposition does not claim actuality, only the absence of necessity.
4
Check the alternative interpretation
The commentator notes that the general possible can also be defined as negating essential impossibility from the agreeing side. These two formulations are equivalent.
For affirmative possibility: absence of impossibility of affirmation = absence of necessity of negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة: الممكنة العامة، وهي التي حكم فيها بسلب الضرورة المطلقة عن الجانب المخالف للحكم، فإن كان الحكم في القضية بالإيجاب كان مفهوم الإمكان سلب ضرورة السلب، لأن الجانب المخالف للإيجاب هو السلب، وإن كان الحكم في القضية بالسلب كان مفهومه سلب ضرورة الإيجاب ، فإنّه هو الجانب المخالف للسلب، فإذا قلنا : «كل نار حارة بالإمكان العام كان معناه أن سلب الحرارة عن النار ليس بضروري؛ وإذا قلنا : «لاشيء من الحارّ ببارد بالإمكان العام»، فمعناه أن : (١) الإمكان العام يفسر تارة بسلب الضرورية الذاتية عن الجانب المخالف للحكم كما ذكره، وتارة بسلب الامتناع الذاتي عن الجانب الموافق، فإمكان الإيجاب معناه عدم امتناع الإيجاب أو عدم ضرورة السلب؛ وكذا الحال في إمكان السلب والتفسيران متساويان كما لا يخفى (شريف).
The sixth: the general possible, which is that in which judgment is made of the negation of absolute necessity from the side opposite to the judgment. For if the judgment in the proposition is affirmative, the concept of possibility is the negation of necessity of negation, because the side opposite to affirmation is negation. If the judgment in the proposition is negative, its concept is the negation of necessity of affirmation, for it is the side opposite to negation. Thus if we say: 'Every fire is hot with general possibility', its meaning is that the negation of heat from fire is not necessary. And if we say: 'Nothing of the hot is cold with general possibility', its meaning is that: (1) The general possible is interpreted sometimes as the negation of essential necessity from the side opposite to the judgment as he mentioned, and sometimes as the negation of essential impossibility from the side agreeing with the judgment. So possibility of affirmation means the absence of impossibility of affirmation or the absence of necessity of negation. The same applies to possibility of negation, and the two interpretations are equivalent, as is not hidden. (Sharif).
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage defines the 'general possible' (al-mumkina al-'amma) proposition, the sixth type. It is a proposition in which the judgment negates absolute necessity from the side opposite to the judgment. That is, if the judgment is affirmative, the possibility means that the negation of the predicate is not necessary; conversely, if the judgment is negative, possibility means that the affirmation is not necessary. Examples: 'Every fire is hot with general possibility' means that the negation of heat from fire is not necessary. 'Nothing of the hot is cold with general possibility' means that the affirmation of coldness for the hot is not necessary. The commentator (Sharif) then adds a note: the general possible is interpreted in two ways. One is as above: negating essential necessity from the opposite side. The other is negating essential impossibility from the agreeing side. For affirmative possibility, it means either the absence of impossibility of affirmation or the absence of necessity of negation; these two formulations are equivalent. The same holds for negative possibility. The two interpretations are logically equivalent, as is evident.

Key Terms
الممكنة العامة
سلب الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The general possible proposition negates absolute necessity from the side opposite to the judgment, whether that judgment is affirmative or negative.

Defining the General Possible (al-Mumkina al-'Amma)
Author defines general possible and gives examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the general possible modality in terms of negating absolute necessity from the side opposite to the judgment and apply it to affirmative and negative propositions.

Core Idea
General possible negates the necessity of the opposite, not the proposition itself.

The general possible is one of the six modalities in Islamic logic, crucial for understanding modal propositions in kalam arguments about contingency and necessity. The passage defines it precisely as judging by the negation of absolute necessity from the side opposite the judgment. Let's break down this definition step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The passage defines 'al-mumkina al-'amma' (the general possible). Note that it is called 'possible' because it contains the meaning of possibility, and 'general' because it is broader than the special possible.
2
Extract the definition's core
The definition is: 'salb al-darura al-mutlaqa 'an al-janib al-mukhalif' (negation of absolute necessity from the opposite side). The 'opposite side' depends on the quality of the judgment: for affirmative, the opposite is negative; for negative, the opposite is affirmative.
3
Apply to the examples
For 'Every fire is hot with general possibility', the opposite is 'some fire is not hot', and its necessity is negated. For 'Nothing hot is cold with general possibility', the opposite is 'some hot thing is cold', and its necessity is negated.
The examples illustrate how the definition works in practice.
4
Distinguish from other uses of 'possible'
General possible does not mean 'probable' or 'contingent' in a probabilistic sense. It strictly means the opposite is not necessarily true. This is a logical modality, not a measure of likelihood.
This avoids a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة: الممكنة العامة، وهي التي حكم فيها بسلب الضرورة المطلقة عن الجانب المخالف للحكم، فإن كان الحكم في القضية بالإيجاب كان مفهوم الإمكان سلب ضرورة السلب، لأن الجانب المخالف للإيجاب هو السلب، وإن كان الحكم في القضية بالسلب كان مفهومه سلب ضرورة الإيجاب، فإنّه هو الجانب المخالف للسلب، فإذا قلنا : «كل نار حارة بالإمكان العام كان معناه أن سلب الحرارة عن النار ليس بضروري؛ وإذا قلنا : «لاشيء من الحارّ ببارد بالإمكان العام»، فمعناه أن إيجاب البرودة للحار ليس بضروري، وإنما سميت «ممكنة» لاحتوائها على معنى الإمكان، و«عامة» لأنّها أعم من الممكنة الخاصة، وهي أعم من المطلقة العامة، لأنه متى صدق الإيجاب بالفعل فلا أقل من أن لا يكون السلب ضرورياً وسلب ضرورة السلب هو إمكان الإيجاب، فمتى صدق الإيجاب بالفعل صدق الإيجاب بالإمكان ولا ينعكس الجواز أن يكون الإيجاب ممكناً ولا يكون واقعاً أصلاً، وكذلك متى صدق السلب بالفعل لم يكن الإيجاب ضرورياً، وسلب ضرورة الإيجاب هو إمكان السلب، فمتى صدق السلب بالفعل صدق السلب بالإمكان دون العكس الجواز أن يكون السلب ممكناً غير واقع. وأعم من القضايا الباقية، لأن المطلقة العامة أعم منها مطلقاً، والأعم من الأعم أعم.
The sixth: the general possible. It is that in which one judges by negating absolute necessity from the side opposite to the judgment. If the judgment in the proposition is affirmative, the concept of possibility is the negation of the necessity of the negative, because the side opposite to affirmation is negation. If the judgment in the proposition is negative, its concept is the negation of the necessity of the affirmative, because it is the side opposite to negation. So if we say: 'Every fire is hot with general possibility,' its meaning is that the negation of heat from fire is not necessary. And if we say: 'Nothing hot is cold with general possibility,' its meaning is that the affirmation of coldness for hot is not necessary. It is called 'possible' because it contains the meaning of possibility, and 'general' because it is more general than the special possible. And it is more general than the general absolute, because whenever an affirmative is actually true, then at the very least the negative is not necessary, and the negation of the necessity of the negative is the possibility of the affirmative. So whenever the affirmative is actually true, the affirmative in the mode of possibility is true, but the converse is not the case, for it is possible that the affirmative is possible but does not actually occur. Likewise, whenever the negative is actually true, the affirmative is not necessary, and the negation of the necessity of the affirmative is the possibility of the negative. So whenever the negative is actually true, the negative in the mode of possibility is true, without the converse, for it is possible that the negative is possible but not actual. And it is more general than the remaining propositions, because the general absolute is more general than it absolutely, and what is more general than the more general is more general.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth modality, the general possible (al-mumkina al-'amma). The author begins by stating that this proposition judges by negating absolute necessity from the side opposite to the judgment. The 'side opposite' is determined by the quality of the judgment: for an affirmative, the opposite is the negative; for a negative, the opposite is the affirmative. Thus the concept of possibility is defined as the negation of the necessity of the opposite. Two examples illustrate: 'Every fire is hot with general possibility' means that the negation of heat from fire is not necessary (i.e., it is not necessarily false that fire is hot). Similarly, 'Nothing hot is cold with general possibility' means that affirming coldness of a hot thing is not necessary. The term 'possible' is used because it contains the meaning of possibility, and 'general' because it is more comprehensive than the special possible. The passage then explains that the general possible is more encompassing than the general absolute: whenever an actual affirmative is true, the affirmative in the possible mode is true, but the converse does not hold (a proposition can be possibly true without being actually true). The same applies to negatives. Finally, it states that the general possible is more general than all the remaining propositions because the general absolute is more general than it, and whatever is more general than the more general is itself the most general. The commentary on the Shamsiyya here expands the original matn, clarifying definitions and relationships through examples and logical implications.

Key Terms
الممكنة العامة
الإمكان العام
الضرورة المطلقة
الجانب المخالف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The proposition is possibly true and possibly false.
B) The negation of the proposition is not necessary.
C) The affirmation is not necessary.
D) The proposition is actually true.

Key Takeaway
General possible is defined by negating the necessity of the opposite, not by any property of the proposition itself.

The Generality Hierarchy of Modal Propositions
Author compares general possible with other modalities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the claims about the generality relationships between the general possible, special possible, and general absolute, and reconcile apparent contradictions in the text.

Core Idea
General possible is broader than special possible and general absolute, but the text's reasoning requires careful parsing.

After defining the general possible, the author makes several statements about its generality: it is more general than the special possible, more general than the general absolute, and more general than the remaining propositions. However, a later statement says the general absolute is more general than it. Let's examine these claims logically.

Guided Path
1
List the generality claims
The passage states: (1) It is called 'general' because it is more general than the special possible. (2) It is more general than the general absolute. (3) It is more general than the remaining propositions. (4) The general absolute is more general than it absolutely.
Claims (2) and (4) seem contradictory.
2
Understand the implication relationship
The text explains that if a proposition is actually true (general absolute), then it is also possible (general possible). This means the set of actually true propositions is a subset of the set of possible propositions. Therefore, general possible has a larger extension than general absolute — that is, it is more general.
This supports claim (2).
3
Parse the later statement
The later statement 'the general absolute is more general than it absolutely' is part of an argument that the general possible is more general than the remaining propositions. The reasoning: the general absolute is more general than the general possible (i.e., it is even more general), and since the general possible is more general than the remaining, the general absolute is the most general. But this uses a different sense of 'more general' — perhaps in terms of applicability? Alternatively, it might be a misinterpretation; the text might actually mean the general often possible is less general.
This contradiction may be resolved by considering the intended hierarchy: general absolute > general possible > special possible > actual. But the text also says general possible > general absolute. This is a puzzle.
4
Reconcile using the text's own logic
Note that the text says 'whenever an affirmative is actually true, the affirmative in the mode of possibility is true, but not conversely.' This implies extension: actually true propositions are a proper subset of possible propositions. Thus, the set of general possible propositions is larger. So 'general possible is more general than general absolute' is correct. The later statement might be a textual error or a different context (e.g., 'general absolute' might refer to a different modality). For this lesson, accept the clear implication from the examples.
This highlights the importance of careful reading in logic.
5
Compare with other modalities
The author asserts that general possible is more general than special possible (the special possible adds the condition of possibility for persistence over time). This is plausible because special possible is more restricted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة: الممكنة العامة، وهي التي حكم فيها بسلب الضرورة المطلقة عن الجانب المخالف للحكم، فإن كان الحكم في القضية بالإيجاب كان مفهوم الإمكان سلب ضرورة السلب، لأن الجانب المخالف للإيجاب هو السلب، وإن كان الحكم في القضية بالسلب كان مفهومه سلب ضرورة الإيجاب، فإنّه هو الجانب المخالف للسلب، فإذا قلنا : «كل نار حارة بالإمكان العام كان معناه أن سلب الحرارة عن النار ليس بضروري؛ وإذا قلنا : «لاشيء من الحارّ ببارد بالإمكان العام»، فمعناه أن إيجاب البرودة للحار ليس بضروري، وإنما سميت «ممكنة» لاحتوائها على معنى الإمكان، و«عامة» لأنّها أعم من الممكنة الخاصة، وهي أعم من المطلقة العامة، لأنه متى صدق الإيجاب بالفعل فلا أقل من أن لا يكون السلب ضرورياً وسلب ضرورة السلب هو إمكان الإيجاب، فمتى صدق الإيجاب بالفعل صدق الإيجاب بالإمكان ولا ينعكس الجواز أن يكون الإيجاب ممكناً ولا يكون واقعاً أصلاً، وكذلك متى صدق السلب بالفعل لم يكن الإيجاب ضرورياً، وسلب ضرورة الإيجاب هو إمكان السلب، فمتى صدق السلب بالفعل صدق السلب بالإمكان دون العكس الجواز أن يكون السلب ممكناً غير واقع. وأعم من القضايا الباقية، لأن المطلقة العامة أعم منها مطلقاً، والأعم من الأعم أعم.
The sixth: the general possible. It is that in which one judges by negating absolute necessity from the side opposite to the judgment. If the judgment in the proposition is affirmative, the concept of possibility is the negation of the necessity of the negative, because the side opposite to affirmation is negation. If the judgment in the proposition is negative, its concept is the negation of the necessity of the affirmative, because it is the side opposite to negation. So if we say: 'Every fire is hot with general possibility,' its meaning is that the negation of heat from fire is not necessary. And if we say: 'Nothing hot is cold with general possibility,' its meaning is that the affirmation of coldness for hot is not necessary. It is called 'possible' because it contains the meaning of possibility, and 'general' because it is more general than the special possible. And it is more general than the general absolute, because whenever an affirmative is actually true, then at the very least the negative is not necessary, and the negation of the necessity of the negative is the possibility of the affirmative. So whenever the affirmative is actually true, the affirmative in the mode of possibility is true, but the converse is not the case, for it is possible that the affirmative is possible but does not actually occur. Likewise, whenever the negative is actually true, the affirmative is not necessary, and the negation of the necessity of the affirmative is the possibility of the negative. So whenever the negative is actually true, the negative in the mode of possibility is true, without the converse, for it is possible that the negative is possible but not actual. And it is more general than the remaining propositions, because the general absolute is more general than it absolutely, and what is more general than the more general is more general.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth modality, the general possible (al-mumkina al-'amma). The author begins by stating that this proposition judges by negating absolute necessity from the side opposite to the judgment. The 'side opposite' is determined by the quality of the judgment: for an affirmative, the opposite is the negative; for a negative, the opposite is the affirmative. Thus the concept of possibility is defined as the negation of the necessity of the opposite. Two examples illustrate: 'Every fire is hot with general possibility' means that the negation of heat from fire is not necessary (i.e., it is not necessarily false that fire is hot). Similarly, 'Nothing hot is cold with general possibility' means that affirming coldness of a hot thing is not necessary. The term 'possible' is used because it contains the meaning of possibility, and 'general' because it is more comprehensive than the special possible. The passage then explains that the general possible is more encompassing than the general absolute: whenever an actual affirmative is true, the affirmative in the possible mode is true, but the converse does not hold (a proposition can be possibly true without being actually true). The same applies to negatives. Finally, it states that the general possible is more general than all the remaining propositions because the general absolute is more general than it, and whatever is more general than the more general is itself the most general. The commentary on the Shamsiyya here expands the original matn, clarifying definitions and relationships through examples and logical implications.

The Special Conditional Proposition
Author defines the special conditional proposition and its composition from two simpler propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the special conditional proposition and decompose it into its two constituent propositions: an unconditional modal and an absolute negation.

Core Idea
The special conditional adds non-permanence by essence to the universal conditional, avoiding contradiction.

In this passage, the author defines the special conditional (al-mashrutat al-khassa), a compound modal proposition that states a predicate holds necessarily as long as a description applies, but not permanently. He explains why the non-permanence must be relative to the subject's essence, not the description, and shows how to construct both affirmative and negative forms from simpler propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and differentia
The special conditional is defined as the universal conditional (genus) plus the qualification of non-permanence according to essence (differentia). Locate these two components in the Arabic text.
The universal conditional is 'necessity according to the description'; non-permanence according to essence means the predicate is not always true of the subject's essence.
2
Understand why non-permanence must be by essence
The author argues that if you tried to qualify the universal conditional with non-permanence according to the description, it would be contradictory because the universal conditional already asserts permanence during the description. Hence non-permanence must be by essence.
This subtle point prevents a logical contradiction and preserves the meaning.
3
Decompose the affirmative example
Take the example: 'Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, but not permanently.' Break it into two parts: (1) the affirmative universal conditional: 'Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer'; (2) the negative universal absolute: 'Nothing of the writer is actually moving fingers' (i.e., it is not always true).
The negative absolute expresses non-permanence.
4
Decompose the negative example
For the negative form: 'Necessarily, not a single writer is with still fingers as long as he is a writer, but not permanently.' The first part is a negative universal conditional; the second part is an affirmative universal absolute that affirms the predicate at some times.
The affirmative absolute here corresponds to the non-permanence of the negation.
5
Test with an Islamic science example
Consider: 'Every mukallaf is accountable as long as he is adult, but not permanently (since accountability ceases with death).' Identify the two parts: universal conditional (accountability during adulthood) and negative absolute (not accountable after death).
This shows how the special conditional handles time-bound rulings in fiqh.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المركبات فسيأتي: الأولى: المشروطة الخاصة، وهي المشروطة العامة مع قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات، وهي إن كانت موجبة - كقولنا: بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع مادام كاتباً لا دائماً - فتركيبها من موجبة مشروطة عامة وسالبة مطلقة عامة، وإن كانت سالبة كقولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع مادام كاتباً لا دائماً - فتركيبها من سالبة مشروطة عامة وموجبة مطلقة عامة. أقول: من المركبات المشروطة الخاصة، وهي المشروطة العامة مع قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات؛ وإنما قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات، لأن المشروطة العامة هي الضرورة بحسب الوصف: والضرورة بحسب الوصف دوام بحسبه؛ والدوام بحسب الوصف يمتنع أن يقيد باللادوام بحسب الوصف، فإن قيد تقييداً صحيحاً فلابد من أن يقيد باللادوام بحسب الذات حتى تكون النسبة فيها ضرورية ودائمة في جميع أوقات وصف الموضوع لا دائمة في بعض أوقات ذات الموضوع، وهي – أعني المشروطة الخاصة - إن كانت موجبة - كقولنا: «بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع مادام كاتباً لا دائماً» فتركيبها من موجبة مشروطة عامة وسالبة مطلقة عامة. أما المشروطة العامة الموجبة فهي الجزء الأول من القضية، وأما السالبة المطلقة العامة فالجزء الثاني من القضية، أي قولنا: «لا شيء من الكاتب بمتحرك الأصابع بالفعل» فهي مفهوم اللادوام، لأن إيجاب المحمول للموضوع إذا لم يكن دائماً كان معناه أن الإيجاب ليس متحققا في جميع الأوقات، وإذالم يتحقق الإيجاب في جميع الأوقات يتحقق السلب في الجملة وهو معنى السالبة المطلقة العامة.
As for compound propositions, they are coming: First: the special conditional (al-mashrutat al-khassa), which is the universal conditional (al-mashrutat al-amma) with the qualification of non-permanence (al-la dawam) according to the essence (bi hasb al-dhat). And if it is affirmative – like our saying: 'Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, but not permanently' – then its composition is from an affirmative universal conditional and a negative universal absolute (mutlaqa amma). And if it is negative – like our saying: 'Necessarily, not a single writer is with still fingers as long as he is a writer, but not permanently' – then its composition is from a negative universal conditional and an affirmative universal absolute. I say: Among compound propositions is the special conditional, which is the universal conditional with the qualification of non-permanence according to the essence. And the reason for qualifying with non-permanence according to the essence is that the universal conditional is necessity according to the description, and necessity according to the description is permanence according to it. And permanence according to the description cannot be qualified with non-permanence according to the description. If it is qualified correctly, it must be qualified with non-permanence according to the essence, so that the relation in it is necessary and permanent in all times of the description of the subject, but not permanent in some times of the essence of the subject. And it – I mean the special conditional – if affirmative – like our saying: 'Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, but not permanently' – then its composition is from an affirmative universal conditional and a negative universal absolute. As for the affirmative universal conditional, it is the first part of the proposition, and as for the negative universal absolute, it is the second part of the proposition, i.e., our saying: 'Nothing of the writer is actually moving fingers.' So it is the concept of non-permanence, because if the affirmation of the predicate for the subject is not permanent, its meaning is that the affirmation is not realized in all times. And if the affirmation is not realized in all times, negation is realized in some way, which is the meaning of the negative universal absolute.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage defines the special conditional proposition, one of the compound modal propositions. The author explains its composition: it is the universal conditional (which asserts necessity during the time of the description) plus a qualification of non-permanence with respect to the essence of the subject. For example, 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer, but not permanently.' The 'not permanently' means that the predicate is not true of the subject in all times of its existence, only during the time of the description. The author clarifies why the non-permanence must be according to essence, not description: if you tried to qualify the universal conditional (which is already permanent with respect to the description) with non-permanence according to the description, that would be contradictory. So it must be according to essence. The composition is broken down: for the affirmative case, the first part is an affirmative universal conditional (e.g., 'Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer'), and the second part is a negative universal absolute that denies the predicate in some times (e.g., 'Nothing of the writer is actually moving fingers' at some times). This second part expresses the non-permanence. The author emphasizes that if the affirmation is not permanent, then negation holds in some times, which is the meaning of the negative universal absolute. Thus the special conditional is a conjunctive proposition combining these two simpler propositions.

The special conditional is a compound proposition that adds a non-permanence condition to the universal conditional, and this non-permanence must be according to essence to avoid logical contradiction.
