Definition and Composition of the Special Conditional
Defines the special conditional and explains its composition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a special conditional proposition and decompose it into its two constituent parts: a general conditional and a general absolute opposite in quality.

Core Idea
The special conditional combines descriptive necessity with an absolute negation to express non-permanence in essence.

The special conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa) is a compound modal proposition used to express that a predicate holds for a subject as long as a certain description applies, but not permanently with respect to the subject's essence. This lesson will walk you through its definition, composition, and how to recognize it in both affirmative and negative forms. We will use an Islamic example from fiqh to ground the concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the special conditional in a sentence
Look for a proposition that states a predicate holds with necessity as long as a description lasts, but also explicitly indicates it is not permanent with respect to the essence. The phrase 'not permanently' (لا دائماً) is a key indicator.
Example: 'Necessarily, every praying person is facing the qibla as long as he is praying, not permanently.'
2
Extract the two component propositions
Separate the compound into a general conditional (the 'as long as' part) and a general absolute (the 'not permanently' part). The general conditional is a simple modal proposition; the general absolute is a non-modal proposition that negates the permanence.
For the affirmative special conditional, the first part is an affirmative general conditional, and the second part is a negative general absolute.
3
Verify the quality of the compound
Determine whether the special conditional is affirmative or negative by looking only at the first part. If the first part (the general conditional) is affirmative, the whole is affirmative; if negative, the whole is negative. The second part always has the opposite quality.
This convention avoids confusion since the compound contains both affirmation and negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات؛ وإنما قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات، لأنّ المشروطة العامة هي الضرورة بحسب الوصف : والضرورة بحسب الوصف دوام بحسبه؛ والدوام بحسب الوصف يمتنع أن يقيد باللادوام بحسب الوصف، فإن قيّد تقييداً صحيحاً فلابد من أن يقيد باللادوام بحسب الذات حتى تكون النسبة فيها ضرورية ودائمة في جميع أوقات وصف الموضوع لادائمة في بعض أوقات ذات الموضوع، وهي – أعني المشروطة الخاصة - إن كانت موجبة - كقولنا : «بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع مادام كاتباً لادائماً » فتركيبها من موجبة مشروطة عامة وسالبة مطلقة عامة. أما المشروطة العامة الموجبة فهي الجزء الأول من القضية، وأما السالبة المطلقة العامة فالجزء الثاني من القضية، أي قولنا : «لاشيء من الكاتب بمتحرك الأصابع بالفعل فهي مفهوم اللادوام، لأن إيجاب المحمول للموضوع إذا لم يكن دائماً كان معناه أن الإيجاب ليس متحققا في جميع الأوقات، وإذالم يتحقق الإيجاب في جميع الأوقات يتحقق السلب في الجملة وهو معنى السالبة المطلقة العامة.
(۱) اعلم أن المشروطة العامة يمكن تقييدها باللاضرورة الذاتية، لكنه تركيب غير معتبر، ويمكن تقييدها باللادوام الذاتي كما ذكره؛ ولا يمكن تقييدها باللاضرورة الوصفية - و هو ظاهر - ولا باللادوام الوصفي و لابسلب الإطلاق العام ولا بسلب الإمكان العام لأنها أعم من الضرورة الوصفية، ولا يجوز تقييد الخاص بسلب العام فإنّه تقييد غير صحيح؛ وقس على ماذكرنا حال سائر المركبات، فيظهر لك أن للتركيب هناك وجوهاً كثيرة : منها ماليس بصحيح، ومنها ماهو صحيح لكنه غير معتبر، ومنها ماهو صحيح ومعتبر (شريف).
وإن كانت سالبة - كقولنا : «بالضرورة لاشيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع مادام كاتباً لا دائماً» - فتركيبها من مشروطة عامة سالبة وهي الجزء الأول، وموجبة مطلقة عامة - أي قولنا : «كل كاتب ساكن الأصابع بالفعل» و هو مفهوم اللادوام ، لأن السلب إذا لم يكن دائماً لم يكن متحققا في جميع الأوقات، وإذا لم يتحقق السلب في جميع الأوقات يتحقق الإيجاب في الجملة، وهو الإيجاب المطلق العام.
فإن قلت: حقيقة القضيّة المركبة ملتئمة من الإيجاب والسلب فكيف تكون موجبة وسالبة ؟
فنقول : الاعتبار في إيجاب القضيّة المركبة وسلبها بإيجاب الجزء الأول وسلبه اصطلاحاً؛ فإن كان الجزء الأول موجباً كانت القضية موجبة، وإن كان سالياً فسالبة؛ والجزء الثاني موافق له في الكم ومخالف له في الكيف.
والنسبة بينها وبين القضايا البسيطة
أما بينها وبين الدائمتين فمباينة كلّية، لأنها مقيدة باللادوام بحسب الذات، وهو مباين للدوام بحسب الذات - وذلك ظاهر - وللضرورة بحسب الذات، لأنّ الضرورة بحسب الذات أخص من الدوام بحسب الذات، ونقيض الأعم مباين لعين الأخص مباينة كلية.
وهي أخص من المشروطة العامة مطلقا لأنّها المشروطة العامة المقيّدة باللادوام والمقيد أخص من المطلق، وكذا من القضايا الثلاث الباقية لأنها أعم من المشروطة العامة.
It is restricted by non-permanence with respect to the essence; and it is only restricted by non-permanence with respect to the essence because the general conditional is necessity with respect to the description, and necessity with respect to the description is permanence with respect to it; and permanence with respect to the description cannot be restricted by non-permanence with respect to the description. If it were restricted validly, it must be restricted by non-permanence with respect to the essence so that the relation in it is necessary and permanent in all times of the subject's description, not permanent in some times of the subject's essence. And it – I mean the special conditional – if it is affirmative, like our saying: 'Necessarily, every writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer, not permanently', then its composition is from an affirmative general conditional and a general absolute negative. As for the affirmative general conditional, it is the first part of the proposition; as for the general absolute negative, it is the second part of the proposition, i.e., our saying: 'No writer is a finger-mover in actuality'; and it is the concept of non-permanence, because if the affirmation of the predicate for the subject is not permanent, its meaning is that the affirmation is not realized in all times, and when the affirmation is not realized in all times, negation is realized in some way, and that is the meaning of the general absolute negative.
(1) Know that the general conditional can be restricted by non-necessity with respect to the essence, but this composition is not considered; and it can be restricted by non-permanence with respect to the essence as he mentioned; and it cannot be restricted by non-necessity with respect to the description – that is apparent – nor by non-permanence with respect to the description, nor by the negation of general absolute, nor by the negation of general possibility because it is more general than necessity with respect to the description; and it is not permissible to restrict the specific by the negation of the general, for that is an invalid restriction; and analogize to what we have mentioned the case of the other composites, so it becomes apparent to you that for composition there are many aspects: some are not valid, some are valid but not considered, and some are valid and considered (Sharif).
And if it is negative – like our saying: 'Necessarily, no writer is a finger-stiller as long as he is a writer, not permanently' – then its composition is from a general conditional negative, which is the first part, and a general absolute affirmative – i.e., our saying: 'Every writer is a finger-stiller in actuality'; and it is the concept of non-permanence, because if the negation is not permanent, it is not realized in all times, and when the negation is not realized in all times, affirmation is realized in some way, and that is the general absolute affirmative.
If you say: The reality of the compound proposition is composed of affirmation and negation, so how can it be affirmative or negative? We say: The consideration in affirming and negating the compound proposition is, by convention, based on the affirmation and negation of the first part. If the first part is affirmative, the proposition is affirmative; if it is negative, then it is negative; and the second part agrees with it in quantity and differs in quality.
And the relation between them and the simple propositions: As for between them and the two permanents, it is complete divergence, because they are restricted by non-permanence with respect to the essence, and it diverges from permanence with respect to the essence – that is apparent – and from necessity with respect to the essence, because necessity with respect to the essence is more specific than permanence with respect to the essence, and the negation of the more general diverges from the very same more specific completely. And it is more specific than the general conditional absolutely, because it is the general conditional restricted by non-permanence, and the restricted is more specific than the unrestricted; and similarly from the remaining three propositions because they are more general than the general conditional.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage defines the special conditional proposition (al-mashruta al-khassa) and explains its composition. The author begins by noting that the restriction in the special conditional is by non-permanence with respect to the essence (al-ladawam bihasb al-dhat), not with respect to the description. This is because the general conditional (al-mashruta al-'amma) expresses necessity with respect to the description, which is a kind of permanence. If we tried to restrict that permanence by non-permanence with respect to the description, it would be contradictory. So the only valid restriction is by non-permanence with respect to the essence, which yields a proposition that is necessary and permanent with respect to the description but not permanent in all times of the essence.

Then he explains the composition for the affirmative case. Example: 'Necessarily, every writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer, not permanently.' This compound consists of an affirmative general conditional ('every writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer') and a general absolute negative ('no writer is a finger-mover in actuality'). The negative part expresses the concept of non-permanence, since if the affirmation were not permanent, it means it is not realized in all times, implying some negation.

He adds a note (from Sharif) about valid and invalid restrictions: the general conditional can be restricted by non-necessity or non-permanence with respect to the essence, but not by non-necessity or non-permanence with respect to the description, nor by negations of general absolutes, etc.

For the negative case, example: 'Necessarily, no writer is a finger-stiller as long as he is a writer, not permanently.' Its composition is a negative general conditional as the first part, and a general absolute affirmative ('every writer is a finger-stiller in actuality') as the second, expressing non-permanence of the negation.

A question arises: If a compound proposition contains both affirmation and negation, how can it be labeled affirmative or negative? The answer: by convention, the label is based on the first part. If the first part is affirmative, the whole is affirmative; if negative, the whole is negative. The second part agrees in quantity (universal/particular) but differs in quality (affirmative/negative).

Finally, the relation between the special conditional and simple propositions: It completely diverges from the two 'permanent' propositions (daima and daruriya) because it is restricted by non-permanence. It is more specific than the general conditional because it adds the restriction of non-permanence. It is also more specific than the other three simple propositions (absolute, etc.) because they are more general than the general conditional.

An Islamic example: 'Every praying person is facing the qibla as long as he is praying, not permanently' (affirmative) or 'No praying person is facing away from the qibla as long as he is praying, not permanently' (negative).

Key Terms

المشروطة العامة
المطلقة العامة
اللادوام بحسب الذات

Valid Restrictions and Logical Relations of the Special Conditional
Discusses valid/invalid restrictions on the general conditional and relations to simple propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid from invalid restrictions on the general conditional and explain the logical relations between the special conditional and simple propositions.

Core Idea
The special conditional is more specific than the general conditional and completely diverges from permanent propositions.

The special conditional is not the only possible compound based on the general conditional. The author, following Sharif, enumerates several restrictions, some valid, some invalid, and some valid but not considered. Understanding these helps clarify the precise logical nature of the special conditional. Additionally, we will examine its relations (mubayana, akhassiyya) to the simple modal propositions.

Guided Path
1
List possible restrictions on the general conditional
The general conditional can be restricted by non-necessity with respect to the essence, non-permanence with respect to the essence, non-necessity with respect to the description, non-permanence with respect to the description, negation of general absolute, and negation of general possibility.
These are the options considered by Sharif.
2
Evaluate the validity of each restriction
Use the rule: it is not permissible to restrict the specific (the general conditional, which is itself a specific necessity) by the negation of a more general modality. Restrictions with respect to essence are valid; restrictions with respect to description are invalid because they contradict the descriptive permanence.
Valid: non-necessity essence, non-permanence essence. Invalid: non-necessity description, non-permanence description, negation of general absolute, negation of general possibility.
3
Compare the special conditional to simple propositions
The special conditional completely diverges (mubayana) from the two permanent propositions (al-daima, al-darura al-dhatiyya). It is more specific (akhass) than the general conditional because it adds the restriction of non-permanence. It is also more specific than the other three simple propositions (absolute, possible, etc.) because they are more general than the general conditional.
This comparison helps locate the special conditional within the hierarchy of modal propositions.

Commentary
This passage defines the special conditional proposition (al-mashruta al-khassa) and explains its composition. The author begins by noting that the restriction in the special conditional is by non-permanence with respect to the essence (al-ladawam bihasb al-dhat), not with respect to the description. This is because the general conditional (al-mashruta al-'amma) expresses necessity with respect to the description, which is a kind of permanence. If we tried to restrict that permanence by non-permanence with respect to the description, it would be contradictory. So the only valid restriction is by non-permanence with respect to the essence, which yields a proposition that is necessary and permanent with respect to the description but not permanent in all times of the essence.

Then he explains the composition for the affirmative case. Example: 'Necessarily, every writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer, not permanently.' This compound consists of an affirmative general conditional ('every writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer') and a general absolute negative ('no writer is a finger-mover in actuality'). The negative part expresses the concept of non-permanence, since if the affirmation were not permanent, it means it is not realized in all times, implying some negation.

He adds a note (from Sharif) about valid and invalid restrictions: the general conditional can be restricted by non-necessity or non-permanence with respect to the essence, but not by non-necessity or non-permanence with respect to the description, nor by negations of general absolutes, etc.

For the negative case, example: 'Necessarily, no writer is a finger-stiller as long as he is a writer, not permanently.' Its composition is a negative general conditional as the first part, and a general absolute affirmative ('every writer is a finger-stiller in actuality') as the second, expressing non-permanence of the negation.

A question arises: If a compound proposition contains both affirmation and negation, how can it be labeled affirmative or negative? The answer: by convention, the label is based on the first part. If the first part is affirmative, the whole is affirmative; if negative, the whole is negative. The second part agrees in quantity (universal/particular) but differs in quality (affirmative/negative).

Finally, the relation between the special conditional and simple propositions: It completely diverges from the two 'permanent' propositions (daima and daruriya) because it is restricted by non-permanence. It is more specific than the general conditional because it adds the restriction of non-permanence. It is also more specific than the other three simple propositions (absolute, etc.) because they are more general than the general conditional.

An Islamic example: 'Every praying person is facing the qibla as long as he is praying, not permanently' (affirmative) or 'No praying person is facing away from the qibla as long as he is praying, not permanently' (negative).

Composition of Compound Propositions and Their Classification
Author explains composition of compound propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two parts of a compound proposition, determine its affirmative/negative classification based on the first part, and compare it to simple propositions like the two permanent ones.

Core Idea
Compound propositions are classified by their first part, not by the overall mixture of affirmation and negation.

In this passage from the logic text, the author explains how compound propositions are built from a conditional part and an absolute part, and why a proposition composed of both affirmation and negation can still be called affirmative or negative. Understanding this structure is crucial for distinguishing compound propositions from simple ones in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example and its parts
Examine the negative compound example: 'Necessarily, no writer is still-fingered as long as he is writing, not always.' Break it into a general conditional negative part and a universal absolute affirmative part.
The first part is the conditional negative; the second is the absolute affirmative.
2
Understand the logic of non-permanence
Follow the reasoning: if the negation is not permanent (i.e., not true at all times), then at times when it is false, the opposite (affirmation) holds at least sometimes, yielding a universal absolute affirmative.
Non-permanence (lā dawām) of negation implies some affirmation.
3
Classify the compound based on the first part
Apply the rule: the compound's quality (affirmative/negative) is determined solely by the first part's quality. The second part opposes in quality but matches in quantity.
This convention is key to understanding the overall classification.
4
Compare with simple propositions
Note that these compounds are completely divergent from the two permanent propositions (always affirmative/negative) because they are restricted by non-permanence. They are more specific than the general conditional.
This situates compound propositions within the broader classification of propositions in Arabic logic.

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the composition of a specific type of compound proposition, focusing on the case where the compound is negative. The author uses the example: 'Necessarily, no writer is still-fingered as long as he is writing, not always.' This is a negative compound proposition composed of a general conditional negative (the first part) and a universal absolute affirmative (the second part), which represents the concept of non-permanence. The logic is that if the negation is not permanent (i.e., not true at all times), then in the times when the negation does not hold, the opposite (affirmation) holds at least sometimes, yielding a universal absolute affirmative. The author then addresses a potential objection: how can a compound proposition, which is composed of both affirmation and negation, itself be categorized as affirmative or negative? The answer is that the classification is based solely on the first part: if the first part is affirmative, the whole is affirmative; if negative, the whole is negative. The second part always opposes the first in quality (affirmation vs. negation) but matches in quantity (both universal or both particular). The compound propositions under discussion are completely divergent from the two permanent propositions because they are restricted by non-permanence (lā dawām) by essence, which is different from permanence. They are more specific than the general conditional because they add the restriction of non-permanence. Thus, they are a subset of the general conditional. The author also notes that these compound propositions are more general than the remaining three simple propositions, likely referring to the particular conditional, etc., because they are more general than the general conditional (since they add a restriction? There might be an OCR or textual error. The intended meaning is likely that these compounds are more specific (أخص) than the general conditional. This confusion should be noted.

The Particular Customary Proposition: Definition and Logical Relations
Author defines the particular customary proposition as the general customary with non-permanence, and explains its composition and relationships.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the particular customary proposition, compose its affirmative and negative forms, and compare its extension with the particular conditional and general conditional.

Core Idea
The particular customary proposition is a general customary plus non-permanence; it is more general than the particular conditional.

In this lesson, we delve into the particular customary proposition (al-'urfiyya al-khāṣṣa), a compound proposition that adds a condition of non-permanence to the general customary. We will see how it is constructed for affirmative and negative cases, and how it relates to other proposition types like the particular conditional and the general conditional.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the particular customary
The definition: it is the general customary (al-'urfiyya al-'āmma) with the condition of non-permanence (lā dawām) according to essence. The general customary expresses a condition that holds as long as the subject is described, e.g., 'Every writer is moving fingers as long as he is writing.' The particular customary adds that this does not hold permanently.
2
Compose the affirmative form
For an affirmative particular customary, combine a general customary affirmative with a universal absolute negative (the concept of non-permanence, i.e., 'not always'). Example: 'Every writer is moving fingers as long as he is writing, not always.'
The first part is the general customary affirmative; the second part is the absolute negative.
3
Compose the negative form
For a negative particular customary, combine a general customary negative with a universal absolute affirmative (the concept of non-permanence). Example: 'No writer is still-fingered as long as he is writing, not always.'
The first part is the general customary negative; the second part is the absolute affirmative.
4
Compare with the particular conditional
The particular customary is more general (a'amm) than the particular conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa). Reasoning: If necessity according to description with non-permanence is true, then permanence according to description with non-permanence is true (so particular conditional implies particular customary). The converse does not hold.
This means the particular customary has a wider extension.
5
Compare with the general conditional and the two permanents
The particular customary diverges from the two permanent propositions entirely. It is more general than the general conditional from one aspect: they coincide in the matter of the particular conditional, but each can be true without the other in other matters (mutual generality-and-specificity).
This relation is known as 'عموم وخصوص من وجه'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥٦ - من القضايا المركبة: العرفية الخاصة
قال : الثانية العرفية الخاصة، وهي العرفية العامة مع قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات وهي إن كانت موجبة فتركيبها من موجبة عرفية عامة وسالبة مطلقة عامة، وإن كانت سالبة فتركيبها من سالبة عرفية عامة وموجبة مطلقة عامة، ومثالها إيجاباً وسلباً مامر.
القول : العرفية الخاصة
مي: العرفية العامة مع قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات؛ وهي إن كانت موجبة - كما مرّ من قولنا : «كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع مادام كاتباً لادائماً» - فتركيبها من موجبة عرفية عامة - وهي
الجزء الأول - وسالبة مطلقة عامة وهي مفهوم اللادوام
وإن كانت سالبة - كما تقدّم من قولنا : «لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع مادام كاتباً لا دائماً » - فتركيبها من سالبة عرفية عامة
- وهي الجزء الأول - وموجبة مطلقة عامة – وهي مفهوم اللادوام. وهي أعم من المشروطة الخاصة مطلقاً - لأنه متى صدقت الضرورة بحسب الوصف لادائماً صدق الدوام بحسب الوصف لادائماً من غير
عکس.

بدون المشروطة العامة إذا كان الدوام بحسب الوصف من غير ضرورة.
56. Among the compound propositions: the particular customary. He said: The second is the particular customary, which is the general customary with the condition of non-permanence according to essence. If it is affirmative, its composition is from a general customary affirmative and a universal absolute negative; if it is negative, its composition is from a general customary negative and a universal absolute affirmative. Their examples, affirmative and negative, have passed. The discussion: The particular customary: i.e., the general customary with the condition of non-permanence according to essence. If it is affirmative - as has passed in our saying: 'Every writer is moving fingers as long as he is writing, not always' - its composition is from a general customary affirmative, which is the first part, and a universal absolute negative, which is the concept of non-permanence. If it is negative - as has passed in our saying: 'No writer is still-fingered as long as he is writing, not always' - its composition is from a general customary negative, which is the first part, and a universal absolute affirmative, which is the concept of non-permanence. And it is more general than the particular conditional absolutely - because whenever the necessity according to description, not always, is true, the permanence according to description, not always, is true without the converse. And it diverges from the two permanents as before. And it is more general than the general conditional from one aspect: because they coincide in the matter of the particular conditional, and the general conditional is true without it in the matter of essential necessity, and it is true without the general conditional when there is permanence according to description without necessity.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage defines the particular customary (al-'urfiyya al-khāṣṣa) as a type of compound proposition. It is the general customary (al-'urfiyya al-'āmma) with the additional condition of non-permanence (lā dawām) according to essence. The composition for the affirmative form (e.g., 'Every writer is moving fingers as long as he is writing, not always') consists of a general customary affirmative (the first part) and a universal absolute negative (the second part, representing non-permanence). For the negative form (e.g., 'No writer is still-fingered as long as he is writing, not always'), it consists of a general customary negative (first part) and a universal absolute affirmative (second part). The text then compares the particular customary with other propositions. First, it is more general (a'amm) than the particular conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa) absolutely. The reasoning: if the necessity according to description with non-permanence is true (i.e., a particular conditional is true), then the permanence according to description with non-permanence (i.e., the particular customary) is also true, but the converse does not hold. So the particular customary has a wider extension. Second, it diverges completely from the two permanent propositions (dā'imatān), as previously explained (in the earlier lesson). Third, it is more general than the general conditional from one aspect (min wajh): they coincide in the matter (i.e., cases) of the particular conditional, but the general conditional can be true without the particular customary in cases of essential necessity, and the particular customary can be true without the general conditional when there is permanence according to description without necessity. This means they have a mutual generality-and-specificity relationship (عموم وخصوص من وجه).

مشروطة خاصة

The Special Conditional ('Urfiyya Khāṣṣa): Definition and Composition
Definition and composition of the special conditional proposition with modal relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the special conditional proposition and compare its modal relations with other conditional types.

Core Idea
The special conditional is a general conditional plus non-permanence of the predicate relative to the subject's essence.

In this lesson, we examine the second type of compound modal proposition: the special conditional ('urfiyya khāṣṣa). The passage defines it and explains its composition in both affirmative and negative forms. You will learn how it relates to other conditionals and why the subject's description must be separable. This precision is essential for correctly interpreting conditional statements in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Parse the definition
Identify the two key components: the special conditional is the general conditional ('urfiyya 'āmma) plus the condition of non-permanence with respect to essence (lā dawām bi-ḥasabi l-dhāt). Understand that 'non-permanence' means the predicate does not hold for the subject's essence intrinsically.
2
Analyse the composition
For the affirmative form, the special conditional combines an affirmative general conditional with a negative absolute absolute proposition. For the negative form, it combines a negative general conditional with an affirmative absolute absolute. The absolute absolute propositions are unrestricted temporals.
The composition ensures the non-permanence condition is logically represented.
3
Compare with other modalities
The text compares the special conditional with three other types: the special conditional (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa), the two 'permanents', and the general conditional. Note that it is absolutely more general than the special conditional, opposite to the permanents, and more general than the general conditional in one respect but more specific absolutely.
Use the provided relationships to understand the hierarchy of modal propositions.
4
Verify the description condition
The description (waṣf) of the subject must be separable (mufāriq) from its essence. If it were essential and the predicate clung due to it, the predicate would be permanent, contradicting the non-permanence condition. This ensures the proposition truly captures a temporary state.
This condition distinguishes the special conditional from a universal essential proposition.
5
Construct an example from Islamic jurisprudence
Formulate a special conditional using a fiqh ruling: 'Every traveler may shorten the prayer as long as he is traveling, non-permanently.' This means the permission holds when the description 'traveler' applies, but not essentially or permanently.
Test your example by verifying its composition and the separability of the description.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية العرفية الخاصة، وهي العرفية العامة مع قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات وهي إن كانت موجبة فتركيبها من موجبة عرفية عامة وسالبة مطلقة عامة، وإن كانت سالبة فتركيبها من سالبة عرفية عامة وموجبة مطلقة عامة، ومثالها إيجاباً وسلباً مامر. وهي أعم من المشروطة الخاصة مطلقاً - لأنه متى صدقت الضرورة بحسب الوصف لادائماً صدق الدوام بحسب الوصف لادائماً من غير عكس. ومباينة للدائمتين على ما سلف. وأعم من المشروطة العامة من وجه لتصادقهما في مادة المشروطة الخاصة وصدق المشروطة العامة بدونها في مادة الضرورة الذاتية وصدقها بدون المشروطة العامة إذا كان الدوام بحسب الوصف من غير ضرورة. وأخص من العرفية العامة لأن المقيد أخص من المطلق. كذا من الباقيتين لأنهما أعم من العرفية العامة. واعلم أن وصف الموضوع في المشروطة والعرفية الخاصتين يجب أن يكون وصفاً مفارقاً لذات الموضوع، فإنه لو كان دائماً له ووصف المحمول دائم بدوام وصف الموضوع : كان وصف المحمول دائماً لذات الموضوع، وقد كان لا دائماً بحسب الذات - هذا خلف.
The second is the special conditional, which is the general conditional with the condition of non-permanence with respect to essence. If it is affirmative, its composition is from an affirmative general conditional and a negative absolute absolute. If it is negative, its composition is from a negative general conditional and an affirmative absolute absolute. Its example in affirmation and negation is what passed. It is more general than the special conditional absolute, because whenever necessity with respect to description is true non-permanently, permanence with respect to description is true non-permanently without the converse. And it is opposite to both permanents as previously. And it is more general than the general conditional in one respect, because they coincide in the matter of the special conditional, and the general conditional is true without it in the matter of essential necessity, and it is true without the general conditional when permanence with respect to description is without necessity. And it is more specific than the general conditional because the restricted is more specific than the unrestricted. Likewise from the remaining two because they are more general than the general conditional. Know that the description of the subject in the special conditional and the special conditional must be a separable description from the essence of the subject, for if it were permanent for it and the description of the predicate is permanent with the permanence of the description of the subject, then the description of the predicate would be permanent for the essence of the subject, and it was non-permanent with respect to essence—this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of compound modal proposition: the special conditional ('urfiyya khāṣṣa). It is a compound of the general conditional ('urfiyya 'āmma) with an additional condition of non-permanence (lā dawām) with respect to the essence (bi-ḥasabi l-dhāt). The author explains its composition: for the affirmative, it combines an affirmative general conditional with a negative absolute absolute proposition; for the negative, it combines a negative general conditional with an affirmative absolute absolute. The example is the one previously given: 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, non-permanently.' Then the author compares this modality with others: it is absolutely more general than the special conditional (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa), because whenever the necessity according to description holds non-permanently, then the permanence according to description also holds non-permanently (but not conversely). It is opposite to both 'permanents' (a type of proposition discussed earlier). It is more general than the general conditional in one respect, coinciding in the matter of the special conditional but differing elsewhere. It is more specific than the general conditional because the restricted term is more specific than the unrestricted. Finally, a crucial note: the description of the subject in both the special conditional and the special conditional must be a separable (mufāriq) attribute, not essential. If it were essential and the predicate clung due to the subject's description, then the predicate would be permanent for the subject's essence, contradicting the non-permanence condition.

The Non-Necessary Existential Proposition: Composition and Essence Condition
Definition and composition of the non-necessary existential proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse the composition of an affirmative and negative non-necessary existential proposition and identify its two component propositions.

Core Idea
The non-necessary existential is a compound of an absolute general and a possible general.

In this lesson, we examine the third compound modal proposition: the non-necessary existential. The author defines it as the absolute general with a condition of non-necessity with respect to essence. We'll break down its composition for both affirmative and negative forms, and understand why the condition is tied to essence rather than description.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall structure
The non-necessary existential proposition is a compound of two simpler propositions: an absolute general (muṭlaqa 'āmma) and a possible general (mumkina 'āmma). The condition of non-necessity is attached to the essence of the subject.
The two components are conjoined by 'and' implicitly: the absolute general assert existence, the possible general denies necessity.
2
Parse the affirmative form
In the affirmative example 'Every human is laughing actually, not necessarily,' the first component is an affirmative absolute general: 'Every human is laughing actually.' The second component is a negative possible general: 'Not necessarily' (i.e., it is possible that some human is not laughing).
The negative possible general expresses the non-necessity condition.
3
Parse the negative form
In the negative example 'No human is laughing actually, not necessarily,' the first component is a negative absolute general: 'No human is laughing actually.' The second component is an affirmative possible general: 'Not necessarily' (i.e., it is possible that some human is laughing).
The affirmative possible general ensures the non-necessity is from the perspective of possibility, not actuality.
4
Understand the 'essence' qualification
The author specifies that the non-necessity condition is 'with respect to essence' (bi-ḥasabi l-dhāt), not 'with respect to description' (bi-ḥasabi l-waṣf). Although the latter could be applied, logicians did not consider that composition nor examined its rulings.
This distinction ensures the modality directly attaches to the subject itself, not to a contingent attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة الوجودية اللاضرورية، وهي المطلقة العامة مع قيد اللاضرورة بحسب الذات وهي إن كانت موجبة - كقولنا: «كلّ إنسان ضاحك بالفعل لا بالضرورة - فتركيبها من موجبة مطلقة عامة، وسالبة ممكنة عامة؛ وإن كانت سالبة - كقولنا: لاشيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل لا بالضرورة»، فتركيبها من سالبة مطلقة عامة وموجبة ممكنة عامة. وإنما قيد اللاضرورة بحسب الذات - وإن أمكن تقييد المطلقة العامة باللاضرورة بحسب الوصف - لأنهم لم يعتبروا هذا التركيب ولم يتعرفوا أحكامه.
The third is the non-necessary existential, which is the absolute general with the condition of non-necessity with respect to essence. It is, if affirmative, as in our saying: 'Every human is laughing actually, not necessarily' – its composition is from an affirmative absolute general and a negative possible general. And if negative, as in our saying: 'No human is laughing actually, not necessarily' – its composition is from a negative absolute general and an affirmative possible general. And the condition of non-necessity with respect to essence is inserted – although it is possible to qualify the absolute general by non-necessity with respect to description – because they did not consider this composition and did not recognize its rulings.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage defines the third compound modal proposition: the non-necessary existential (wujūdiyya lā-ḍarūriyya). It is the absolute general (muṭlaqa 'āmma) with an added condition of non-necessity with respect to essence (lā-ḍarūra bi-ḥasabi l-dhāt). The author gives two examples: affirmative – 'Every human is laughing actually, not necessarily'; negative – 'No human is laughing actually, not necessarily'. The composition: for affirmative, an affirmative absolute general (the first part) combined with a negative possible general (the second part, conveying the non-necessity); for negative, a negative absolute general combined with an affirmative possible general. The author then explains why they specify 'with respect to essence' rather than 'with respect to description': although it would be possible to qualify the absolute general by non-necessity with respect to description, the logicians did not consider that composition nor examine its rulings. So the standard treatment is with respect to essence. This highlights that the compound modal propositions are carefully constructed to capture precise temporal and essential conditions.

The Existential Non-Permanent Proposition: Definition and Composition
Defines the fourth compound proposition, its composition, and comparisons with other modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the existential non-permanent proposition from two general absolutes and identify its logical relationships with other modal propositions.

Core Idea
The existential non-permanent is a compound of two absolute propositions: one affirms, the other negates permanence.

In this passage, the author defines the fourth compound proposition, the existential non-permanent (الوجودية اللادائمة). This proposition is built from two general absolute propositions, expressing that a predicate actually holds but not permanently with respect to the essence. Understanding its composition and how it relates to other modalities is essential for mastering modal logic in Islamic kalam and usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Locate the term 'الوجودية اللادائمة' (existential non-permanent) and note that it is the fourth compound proposition being defined.
The term appears at the beginning of the passage.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'المطلقة العامة' (general absolute) with the condition of non-permanence (قيد اللادوام). The differentia is 'بحسب الذات' (with respect to essence).
The definition is 'هي المطلقة العامة مع قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات'.
3
Analyze the composition
The proposition consists of two general absolutes: one affirmative and one negative. The first part is the general absolute (actuality of relation), the second part is the negation of permanence, which itself is a general absolute in meaning.
The text states: 'تركيبها من مطلقتين عامتين: إحداهما موجبة، والأخرى سالبة'.
4
Examine the examples
The examples are 'كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل لا دائماً' (affirmative) and 'لاشيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل لا دائماً' (negative). Note the structure: actuality + 'not always'.
These examples illustrate the composition.
5
Compare with other modalities
Work through the comparisons: it is more specific than existential non-necessary, more general than the two particular ones, disparate from the two permanents, etc. Understanding these relations helps locate this proposition in the modal system.
Each comparison uses a specific logical relationship (أخص, أعم, مباينة).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: الرابعة الوجودية اللادائمة، وهي المطلقة العامة مع قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات وهي سواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة، فتركيبها من مطلقتين عامتين: إحداهما موجبة، والأخرى سالبة، ومثالها إيجاباً وسلباً مامر. القول : الوجودية اللادائمة هي المطلقة العامة مع قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات، وهي - سواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة - يكون تركيبها من مطلقتين عامتين إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة؛ لأن الجزء الأول مطلقة عامة والجزء الثاني هو اللادوام ، وقد عرفت أن مفهومه مطلقة عامة. ومثالها إيجاباً وسلباً مامر من قولنا : «كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل لا دائماً» و «لاشيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل لادائماً». وهي أخص من الوجودية اللاضرورية، لأنه متى صدقت مطلقتان صدقت مطلقة وممكنة، بخلاف العكس. وأعم من الخاصتين، لأنه متى تحقق الضرورة أو الدوام بحسب الوصف لادائماً، تحقق فعلية النسبة لادائماً من غير عكس. ومباينة للدائمتين - على مامر غير مرة. وأعم من العامتين من وجه لتصادقها في مادة المشروطة الخاصة وصدقهما بدونها في مادة الضرورة، وبالعكس حيث لادوام بحسب الوصف. وأخص من المطلقة والممكنة العامتين، وذلك ظاهر.
He said: The fourth is the existential non-permanent, which is the general absolute with the condition of non-permanence in respect of essence, and it is whether affirmative or negative. Its composition is from two general absolutes: one affirmative and the other negative. Its example, both affirmative and negative, is what has passed. The statement: The existential non-permanent is the general absolute with the condition of non-permanence in respect of essence, and it - whether affirmative or negative - its composition is from two general absolutes, one affirmative and the other negative; because the first part is a general absolute and the second part is non-permanence, and you have known that its concept is a general absolute. Its example, both affirmative and negative, is what has passed from our saying: "Every human is laughing in actuality, not always" and "Nothing of the human is laughing in actuality, not always." And it is more specific than the existential non-necessary, because whenever two absolutes are true, an absolute and a possible are true, unlike the converse. And it is more general than the two particular ones, because whenever the necessary or the permanent in respect of description, non-permanently, materializes, the actuality of the relation materializes non-permanently without converse. And it is disparate from the two permanents - as has passed multiple times. And it is more general than the two general ones in one respect, because they coincide in the matter of the specific conditional and they are true without it in the matter of necessity, and conversely where there is no permanence in respect of description. And it is more specific than the general absolute and the general possible, and that is apparent.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of compound proposition, the existential non-permanent (الوجودية اللادائمة). It begins by stating that it is the general absolute (المطلقة العامة) with the additional condition of non-permanence (اللادوام) with respect to the essence (بحسب الذات). The proposition can be affirmative or negative. Its composition consists of two general absolute propositions: one affirmative and one negative. The first part is the general absolute (the actuality of the relation), and the second part is the negation of permanence, which itself is a general absolute proposition in meaning. The examples given are: 'Every human is laughing in actuality, not always' (affirmative) and 'Nothing of the human is laughing in actuality, not always' (negative). The passage then compares this proposition with other modal propositions. It is more specific (أخص) than the existential non-necessary (الوجودية اللاضرورية) because if two absolutes are true (i.e., an absolute and a possible), the existential non-permanent is true, but not conversely. It is more general (أعم) than the two particular ones (الخاصتين) because whenever necessity or permanence in respect of description holds non-permanently, the actuality of the relation holds non-permanently, but not conversely. It is disparate (مباينة) from the two permanents (الدائمتين). It is more general than the two general ones (العامتين) in one aspect because they coincide in the matter of the specific conditional (المشروطة الخاصة) but diverge in necessity and non-permanence. Finally, it is more specific than the general absolute and the general possible (المطلقة والممكنة العامتين). This classification helps in understanding the precise logical relationships between different types of modal propositions.

المطلقة العامة
اللادوام

D) One absolute and one non-necessary.

Key Takeaway
The existential non-permanent is a compound modal proposition that explicitly negates permanence, built from two general absolutes.

The Temporal Proposition: Definition and Composition
Definition and composition of the temporal proposition with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the temporal proposition and decompose it into its two component parts (affirmative/negative temporal absolute and general absolute).

Core Idea
The temporal proposition marries necessity at a specific time with a condition of non-permanence.

We now encounter the temporal proposition, which is a compound modal proposition used to express that a predicate holds necessarily for a subject at a specific time, but not permanently. This type of proposition is crucial in Islamic sciences — for example, when a mutakallim (theologian) argues that the world is temporally originated at the time of its creation but does not continue to be originated thereafter. In this lesson, we will dissect its definition, examine its two examples from the text, and understand its internal composition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic passage. It is 'الوقتية' (al-waqtiyya). Note that the author immediately provides a definition: it is a judgment of necessary affirmation or negation at a specific time of the subject's existence, with the condition of non-permanence.
The term 'temporal' refers to the time-bound nature of the necessity.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'modal proposition' (or 'compound modal proposition'). The differentia is the restriction to a specific time (وقت معين) and the condition of non-permanence (اللادوام). Together they distinguish the temporal from other modal propositions like the absolute or the possible.
The differentia here is two-part: time specificity and non-permanence.
3
Analyse the affirmative example
Examine the affirmative temporal proposition: 'بالضرورة كل قمر منخسف وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس لادائماً'. Decompose it into its two parts: (1) the affirmative temporal absolute 'كل قمر منخسف وقت الحيلولة' and (2) the negative general absolute 'لاشيء من القمر بمنخسف بالإطلاق العام'. Recognise that the second part expresses the non-permanence condition.
The first part is the temporal core; the second part denies permanence.
4
Analyse the negative example
Now look at the negative temporal proposition: 'بالضرورة لاشيء من القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع لادائماً'. Its parts are: (1) the negative temporal absolute 'لاشيء من القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع' and (2) the affirmative general absolute 'كل قمر منخسف بالإطلاق العام'.
Note how the second part flips polarity compared to the affirmative case.
5
Understand the specificity claim
The author ends by stating that the temporal is more specific than the two existential propositions (general and non-permanent). Check this: if the temporal 'necessary at time not always' is true, then the absolute 'not always' is also true, but the converse does not hold. This highlights the unique modal force of the temporal.
This logical relationship is important for inferential reasoning.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage defines the temporal proposition (الوقتية), the fifth type of compound modal. The author first gives a definition: it is a proposition that judges the necessary affirmation or negation of a predicate for a subject at a specific time within the subject's existence, qualified by non-permanence. Two examples are provided: affirmative 'Necessarily every moon is eclipsed at the time of earth's interposition not always' and negative 'Necessarily no moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature not always.' The composition is then detailed: if affirmative, it consists of an affirmative temporal absolute (the first part) and a negative general absolute (the concept of non-permanence); if negative, it consists of a negative temporal absolute and an affirmative general absolute. The author also explains that the temporal is more specific than the two existential propositions (general and non-permanent) because the truth of the temporal with non-permanent necessity implies the truth of the absolute (non-permanent) but not vice versa, and the implication does not reverse. The lunar eclipse examples from astronomy are used to illustrate the specific time condition.

Definition and Composition of the Temporal Proposition
Author defines and decomposes the temporal proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining features of a temporal modal proposition (al-waqtiyya) and decompose its affirmative and negative forms into their constituent parts.

Core Idea
Every temporal proposition combines a time-specific necessity with a universal absolute negation.

The temporal proposition (al-waqtiyya) is a key modal form in Islamic logic. It judges necessity at a specific time during the subject's existence, with an explicit 'not eternally' qualifier. Understanding its composition is essential for analyzing temporal modalities in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of al-waqtiyya
Find the sentence that defines the temporal proposition. Note the key components: judgment of necessity, specific time of the subject's existence, and the qualifier 'la da'iman' (not eternally).
The definition is: 'al-waqtiyya hiya allati hukima fiha bi-darurati thubuti al-mahmul lil-mawdu' aw bi-darurati salbihi 'anhu fi waqt mu'ayyin min awqat wujud al-mawdu' muqayyadan bi-al-ladawam bi-hasab al-dhat.'
2
Extract the composition of an affirmative temporal
The author gives an example: 'Necessarily, every moon is eclipsed at the time of interposition, not eternally.' Break it into two parts: the affirmative temporal part and the universal negative absolute part.
Affirmative temporal part: 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of interposition.' Universal negative absolute: 'No moon is eclipsed in a general absolute sense.'
3
Extract the composition of a negative temporal
Similarly, for a negative example: 'Necessarily, no moon is eclipsed at quadrature, not eternally.' The two parts are a negative temporal and a universal affirmative absolute.
Negative temporal part: 'No moon is eclipsed at quadrature.' Universal affirmative absolute: 'Every moon is eclipsed in a general absolute sense.'
4
Explain the role of 'la da'iman'
The qualifier 'not eternally' ensures that the necessity is not perpetual but restricted to the specified time. It distinguishes the temporal from the eternal necessary.
Without this qualifier, the proposition would imply eternal necessity, which is not intended.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the temporal modal proposition (al-waqtiyya) within the context of modal logic. The author first continues a discussion already in progress, stating that the temporal proposition involves a judgment of necessity (affirmative or negative) at a specific time during the existence of the subject, with the additional qualifier of non-eternality (la dawam) with respect to the essence. He then clarifies the composition of affirmative and negative temporal propositions. An affirmative temporal, such as 'Necessarily, every moon is eclipsed at the time of earth's interposition, not eternally,' is composed of a temporal affirmative part ('Every moon is eclipsed at that time') and a universal negative absolute ('No moon is eclipsed in a general absolute sense'). Similarly, a negative temporal, like 'Necessarily, no moon is eclipsed at quadrature, not eternally,' consists of a temporal negative part ('No moon is eclipsed at quadrature') and a universal affirmative absolute ('Every moon is eclipsed in a general absolute sense'). The author then compares the temporal proposition to other modal types. He states that the temporal is more specific than the two existential propositions (al-wujudiyyat) absolutely, meaning that whenever the temporal is true, the absolute existential (al-mutlaq) is also true but not vice versa; it does not convert. It is more general than the two special propositions (al-khassatan) in one respect: if the necessity with respect to the description holds, and the description itself is necessary for the subject at some time, then three propositions (temporal, special, etc.) are true. For example, 'Necessarily, every eclipsed thing is dark as long as it is eclipsed, not eternally'—since eclipse is necessary for the subject at some time, darkness follows. But if the description is not necessary for the essence (e.g., writing is not necessary for a person), then only the special propositions (e.g., 'Necessarily, every writer moves fingers as long as he writes') are true, but the temporal is not. Conversely, if the necessity is only with respect to time (like the lunar eclipse example), then only the temporal is true, not the specials. Finally, the author discusses different interpretations of the conditional (mashruta). If interpreted as necessity under condition of the description, the special conditional is less specific than the temporal? Actually, the text says that if interpreted as necessity as long as the description exists, then the special conditional is more specific than the temporal absolutely. That section is incomplete but indicates a refinement based on definition. Overall, this passage provides a detailed taxonomy and comparison of modal propositions, essential for understanding logical syllogisms involving temporal modalities.

اللادوام
المطلقة العامة

Comparing Temporal, Existential, and Conditional Propositions
Author compares temporal proposition with existential and conditional types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the temporal proposition with the existential (wujudiyya) and conditional (mashruta) propositions in terms of logical scope and truth conditions.

Core Idea
The temporal is more specific than the existential and has a complex relation with the conditional depending on the necessity of the subject's description.

The author not only defines the temporal proposition but also situates it among other modal types. By understanding how the temporal relates to the existential and conditional propositions, you gain a more precise grasp of modal reasoning, which is essential for evaluating arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim that waqtiyya is more specific than al-wujudiyyat
The author says: 'Wa hiya akhass min al-wujudiyyat mutlaqan.' This means that whenever a temporal proposition is true, the corresponding existential (non-modal) proposition is also true, but not vice versa. The temporal adds both necessity and a time restriction.
For example, if 'Necessarily, every moon is eclipsed at time T, not eternally' is true, then 'Every moon is eclipsed (absolutely)' is also true, but the reverse does not hold.
2
Analyze the relation with the conditional (mashruta) when description is necessary for the subject
If the description is necessary for the subject (e.g., eclipse is necessary for the moon at some time), then the temporal and the conditional can both be true. The author gives the example of 'Every eclipsed thing is dark as long as it is eclipsed' being also temporally true.
Here, the conditional (mashruta) and temporal coincide due to the necessity of the description.
3
Examine the case when the description is not necessary for the subject
If the description (e.g., being a writer) is not necessary for the subject (e.g., a human), then the conditional may be true but the temporal is false. The example: 'Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, not eternally' is true as a conditional but not as a temporal because writing is not a necessary attribute.
This shows the conditional is more general in such cases.
4
Identify the case when only the temporal is true
If the description is not necessary and the necessity is not perpetual, but the judgment holds at a specific time, then only the temporal is true. The lunar eclipse example fits: the eclipse is not a permanent description of the moon, but at the time of interposition, it is necessary.
Thus, the temporal captures a specific temporal necessity not captured by the conditional.

Source Text

للموضوع أو سلبيه عنه في وقت معين من أوقات وجود الموضوع مع قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات وهي إن كانت موجبة كقولك: «بالضرورة كل قمر منخسف وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس لا دائماً»- فتركيبها من موجبة وقتية مطلقة وسالبة مطلقة عامة، وإن كانت سالبة - كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع لا دائماً فتركيبها من سالبة وقتية مطلقة وموجبة مطلقة عامة.
القول : الوقتية هي التي حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو بضرورة سلب عنه في وقت معين من أوقات وجود الموضوع مقيداً باللادوام بحسب الذات.
مطلقة
فإن كانت موجبة - كقولنا : بالضرورة كل قمر منخسف وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس لا دائماً»- فتركيبها من موجبة وقتية وهي الجزء الأول - أي قولنا : كل قمر منخسف وقت الحيلولة» وسالبة مطلقة عامة وهي مفهوم اللادوام - أعني قولنا : «لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف بالإطلاق العام». : وإن كانت سالبة - كقولنا : بالضرورة لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع لا دائماً فتركيبها من سالبة وقتية مطلقة . وهي الجزء الأول - أي قولنا : «لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع» ومن موجبة مطلقة عامة، وهي : «كل قمر منخسف بالإطلاق العام». وهي أخص من الوجوديتين مطلقاً، لأنه إذا صدق الضرورة بحسب الوقت لا دائماً، صدق الإطلاق لا دائماً ولا بالضرورة، ولا تنعكس. وأعم من الخاصتين من وجه، لأنه إذا صدقت الضرورة بحسب الوصف، فإن كان الوصف ضرورياً لذات الموضوع في شيء من الأوقات، صدقت القضايا الثلاث، كقولنا : «بالضرورة كل منخسف مظلم مادام منخسفاً لا دائماً» أو «بالتوقيت لا دائماً، فإن الانخساف لما كان ضرورياً لذات الموضوع في بعض الأوقات - والإظلام ضروري للانخفاس - كان الإظلام ضرورياً للذات في ذلك الوقت. وإن لم يكن الوصف ضرورياً لذات الموضوع في وقت صدقت الخاصتان، ولم تصدق الوقتية، كقولنا : «بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع مادام كاتباً لا دائماً»، فإنّ الكتابة لما لم تكن ضرورية للذات في شيء من الأوقات لم يكن تحرك الأصابع - الضروري بحسبها – ضرورياً للذات في وقت ما، فلا تصدق الوقتية، وإذا لم تصدق الضرورة بحسب الوصف ولا الدوام - وصدقت بحسب الوقت - لم تصدق الخاصتان وتصدق الوقتية كما في المثال المذكور. (١) وهذا إذا فسرنا المشروطة بالضرورة بشرط الوصف، أما إذا فسرناها بالضرورة مادام الوصف : تكون المشروطة الخاصة أخص من الوقتية مطلقا ، لأنه متى تحققت الضرورة في جميع أوقات الوصف وجميع أوقات (١) يعني قوله : «كل قمر منخسف وقت حيلولة الأرض، فإن الانخفاس ليس ضرورتاً بحسب وصف القمرية ولا دائماً بحسبه، فلايصدق «كل قمر منخسف مادام قمراً» (شریف). (٢) وذلك لأن الضرورة المعتبرة في المشروطة الخاصة حينئذ بالقياس إلى ذات الموضوع في زمان الوصف، وذلك وقت معيّن، فتصدق الضرورة الوقتية هناك أيضاً لأنها بالقياس إلى الذات في وقت معين، فكلما صدقت
for the subject or its negation about it at a specific time from the times of existence of the subject with the qualification of non-eternality with respect to the essence. If it is affirmative, like your saying: 'Necessarily, every moon is eclipsed at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, not eternally'—so its composition is from an affirmative temporal absolute and a universal negative absolute. If it is negative, like our saying: 'Necessarily, no moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not eternally'—so its composition is from a negative temporal absolute and a universal affirmative absolute. The statement: The temporal is that in which judgment is made about the necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject or the necessity of its negation from it at a specific time from the times of existence of the subject, qualified by non-eternality with respect to the essence. Absolute. If it is affirmative—like our saying: 'Necessarily, every moon is eclipsed at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, not eternally'—so its composition is from an affirmative temporal, which is the first part—i.e., our saying: 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of interposition'—and a universal negative absolute, which is the concept of non-eternality—I mean our saying: 'No moon is eclipsed by universal absolute.' And if it is negative—like our saying: 'Necessarily, no moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not eternally'—so its composition is from a negative temporal absolute, which is the first part—i.e., our saying: 'No moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature'—and from a universal affirmative absolute, which is: 'Every moon is eclipsed by universal absolute.' And it is more specific than the two existential (propositions) absolutely, because if the necessity with respect to time, not eternally, is true, then the absolute not eternally and not necessarily is true, and it does not convert. And it is more general than the two particular (propositions) in one respect, because if the necessity with respect to the description is true, then if the description is necessary for the essence of the subject at some time, the three propositions are true, like our saying: 'Necessarily, every eclipsed thing is dark as long as it is eclipsed, not eternally' or 'by temporalization, not eternally,' for when the eclipse was necessary for the essence of the subject at some times—and darkness is necessary for the eclipse—then darkness is necessary for the essence at that time. And if the description is not necessary for the essence of the subject at any time, then the two particular (propositions) are true, but the temporal is not true, like our saying: 'Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, not eternally,' for when writing was not necessary for the essence at any time, then the movement of the fingers—which is necessary with respect to it—was not necessary for the essence at any time, so the temporal is not true. (2) That is because the necessity considered in the particular conditional then in relation to the essence of the subject in the time of the description, and that is a specific time, so the temporal necessity is also true there because it is in relation to the essence at a specific time, so whenever it is true
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the temporal modal proposition (al-waqtiyya) within the context of modal logic. The author first continues a discussion already in progress, stating that the temporal proposition involves a judgment of necessity (affirmative or negative) at a specific time during the existence of the subject, with the additional qualifier of non-eternality (la dawam) with respect to the essence. He then clarifies the composition of affirmative and negative temporal propositions. An affirmative temporal, such as 'Necessarily, every moon is eclipsed at the time of earth's interposition, not eternally,' is composed of a temporal affirmative part ('Every moon is eclipsed at that time') and a universal negative absolute ('No moon is eclipsed in a general absolute sense'). Similarly, a negative temporal, like 'Necessarily, no moon is eclipsed at quadrature, not eternally,' consists of a temporal negative part ('No moon is eclipsed at quadrature') and a universal affirmative absolute ('Every moon is eclipsed in a general absolute sense'). The author then compares the temporal proposition to other modal types. He states that the temporal is more specific than the two existential propositions (al-wujudiyyat) absolutely, meaning that whenever the temporal is true, the absolute existential (al-mutlaq) is also true but not vice versa; it does not convert. It is more general than the two special propositions (al-khassatan) in one respect: if the necessity with respect to the description holds, and the description itself is necessary for the subject at some time, then three propositions (temporal, special, etc.) are true. For example, 'Necessarily, every eclipsed thing is dark as long as it is eclipsed, not eternally'—since eclipse is necessary for the subject at some time, darkness follows. But if the description is not necessary for the essence (e.g., writing is not necessary for a person), then only the special propositions (e.g., 'Necessarily, every writer moves fingers as long as he writes') are true, but the temporal is not. Conversely, if the necessity is only with respect to time (like the lunar eclipse example), then only the temporal is true, not the specials. Finally, the author discusses different interpretations of the conditional (mashruta). If interpreted as necessity under condition of the description, the special conditional is less specific than the temporal? Actually, the text says that if interpreted as necessity as long as the description exists, then the special conditional is more specific than the temporal absolutely. That section is incomplete but indicates a refinement based on definition. Overall, this passage provides a detailed taxonomy and comparison of modal propositions, essential for understanding logical syllogisms involving temporal modalities.

Key Terms
الوقتية
الوجوديتان
الخاصتان
المشروطة الخاصة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a temporal proposition true but the corresponding conditional (mashruta) false?
A) When the description is necessary for the subject's essence.
B) When the necessity is with respect to time and the description is not necessary for the subject's essence.
C) When the necessity is perpetual.
D) When the subject does not exist at the specified time.

Key Takeaway
The temporal and conditional propositions overlap in complex ways, depending on whether the description is necessary for the subject's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition 'Necessarily, every human is laughing as long as they are being tickled, not eternally,' determine whether it is a conditional, temporal, both, or neither, using the criteria in the passage.
?
Construct a conditional proposition from a fiqh example (e.g., 'Necessarily, every person in the state of ihram avoids perfume as long as they are in ihram, not eternally') and then test if the corresponding temporal proposition would be true.

Interpreting al-Mashruta: Condition vs Duration
Author discusses two interpretations of the conditional and their effect on scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish two interpretations of the special conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa) and explain how they affect the scope relationship with the temporal proposition.

Core Idea
Interpreting mashruta as 'necessity under condition' vs 'necessity as long as the description' reverses its generality relative to waqtiyya.

The passage ends by noting a critical ambiguity in the definition of the special conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa). Depending on whether it is interpreted as 'necessity under condition of the description' or 'necessity as long as the description exists,' its logical relationship with the temporal proposition changes. This distinction is vital for correct syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations of al-mashruta al-khassa
The author mentions two definitions: (1) necessity under condition of the description (bi-shart al-wasf), and (2) necessity as long as the description exists (ma dama al-wasf). Find the sentence that introduces this distinction.
The text says: 'Hadha idha fassarna al-mashruta bi-al-darura bi-shart al-wasf, amma idha fassarnaha bi-al-darura ma dama al-wasf: takunu al-mashruta al-khassa akhass min al-waqtiyya mutlaqan.'
2
Understand the first interpretation (bi-shart al-wasf)
Under the 'under condition' interpretation, the conditional asserts necessity only when the description is present. The scope relation with the temporal is as discussed earlier: the temporal is more general in some respects.
Example: 'Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers under condition of writing, not eternally.'
3
Understand the second interpretation (ma dama al-wasf)
Under the 'as long as' interpretation, the conditional asserts necessity for the entire duration the description exists. This makes it more specific than the temporal, because if the necessity holds for all times the description exists, it also holds for a specific time within that duration.
Example: 'Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, not eternally.' Here, if the description (writing) is present for a span, the necessity applies throughout, so it implies the temporal at each specific moment of writing.
4
Apply to the lunar eclipse example
Consider 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of interposition.' Under the 'under condition' interpretation, this is not a conditional because the description 'moon' is not a condition that triggers the eclipse. Under the 'as long as' interpretation, it is also not a conditional because there is no description that lasts (eclipse itself is the predicate). The passage uses the moon example to show when the temporal is true but the conditional is not.
The note from Sharif clarifies that the moon's eclipse is not due to the description of moonness but to the specific time.

That section is incomplete but indicates a refinement based on definition. How Description Necessity Determines Modal Truth
Author explains how necessity of description determines truth of three modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the truth of three modal propositions based on whether the description is necessary for the subject's essence.

Core Idea
Modal truth depends on whether the description is essential or accidental to the subject.

In modal logic, the truth of propositions like 'every A is B as long as it is A' depends on the nature of the description. This passage from Qutbi clarifies two cases: when the description is necessary for the essence and when it is not. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence where temporal conditions matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The author presents two scenarios: the description is necessary for the subject's essence at some time, or it is not necessary at any time. Note which case each example illustrates.
2
Determine the truth of the three propositions
For each case, list which of the three propositions (special conditional, temporal, perpetual) are true. The text gives examples: when the description is necessary, all three are true; when not necessary, only the two special ones are true, and the temporal is false.
3
Apply the rule to the eclipse example
For 'eclipsed' – which is necessary for the moon at the time of eclipse – the special conditional 'as long as it is eclipsed' and the temporal 'at that time' both hold. Understand why the perpetual 'always' is false.
4
Apply the rule to the writer example
For 'writer' – not necessary for man at any time – only the special conditional 'as long as he is a writer' holds; the temporal proposition is false because writing is not bound to a specific time.
5
Interpret the commentator's addition
The commentator uses the eclipse at the time of earth's interposition to show that if the description is not necessary for the essence (eclipse is not necessary for moonness), then the proposition 'every moon is eclipsed as long as it is a moon' is false. This reinforces the rule.

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions under which three types of modal propositions are true when the proposition includes a description (wasf) that qualifies the subject. The author first considers the case where the description is necessary for the essence of the subject in some times. For example, 'eclipsed' is a description of the moon that applies only when the moon is actually eclipsed; the essence 'moon' does not always have that description. In this case, the proposition 'Every eclipsed [moon] is dark as long as it is eclipsed' (shartiyya khassa) is true, as is the temporal (waqtiyya) proposition 'at that time', and the possible (mumkina) proposition? Actually the text says 'the three propositions' - likely referring to the special conditional (mashruta khassa), the temporal (waqtiyya), and the perpetual (daima)? But the example shows 'as long as it is eclipsed' (mashruta khassa) and 'by timing' (waqtiyya). Then the second case: when the description is not necessary for the essence at any time, like 'writer' for a human (not all humans are writers), then only the special conditional ('Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer') and the general absolute (mutlaqa 'amma) are true, but the temporal is false because there is no specific time when writing is necessary for the essence. The commentator adds a clarifying note using the example of the moon's eclipse at the time of earth's interposition to show that the description 'eclipsed' is not necessary for moonness, so the proposition 'Every moon is eclipsed as long as it is a moon' is false. This passage teaches how to evaluate modal propositions based on the ontological necessity of the description to the subject's essence.

Two Interpretations of Mashruta and Relations with Waqtiyya
Author compares two interpretations of mashruta and their logical relationships with waqtiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two interpretations of al-mashruta al-khassa and determine when it is more specific than al-waqtiyya.

Core Idea
Interpretation of 'mashruta' as 'as long as' makes it more specific than waqtiyya; 'with condition' does not.

In this passage, the author clarifies the meaning of the special conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa) by exploring two interpretations. One takes it as 'necessity with the condition of the description,' the other as 'necessity as long as the description holds.' Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly mapping relationships among modal propositions in Islamic logic, particularly when analyzing the temporal scope of conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
Find the two ways the author defines al-mashruta al-khassa: (1) necessity with the condition of description, and (2) necessity as long as the description holds.
These are contrasted explicitly in the opening sentences.
2
Determine which yields 'more specific'
The author states that under the second interpretation, the special conditional is more specific than waqtiyya absolutely. Under the first, it is not.
Compare the phrases 'إذا فسرنا المشروطة بالضرورة بشرط الوصف' and 'إذا فسرناها بالضرورة مادام الوصف'.
3
Trace the logical consequence
When necessity is as long as description, it holds at a specific time (the time of description), so it implies the temporal necessity which is also relative to a specific time.
The marginal note explains why the implication holds: both are relative to the essence at a time.
4
Locate waqtiyya in the taxonomy
The temporal is distinct from the two perpetual propositions and more general than the two general propositions in some respects, but more specific than the general absolute and general possible.
This situates waqtiyya relative to the da'imtayn and 'ammatayn.
