This is if we interpret the conditional (mashruta) as necessity with the condition of description. But if we interpret it as necessity as long as the description: then the special conditional is more specific than the temporal (waqtiyya) absolutely, because whenever necessity is realized in all times of the description and all times (2) That is because the necessity considered in the special conditional then is relative to the essence of the subject in the time of the description, and that is a specific time, so temporal necessity is true there as well because it is relative to the essence in a specific time. Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage delves into the nuanced definition of the 'special conditional' (al-mashruta al-khassa) by considering two interpretations. The first interpretation takes 'mashruta' as asserting necessity with the condition of the description (e.g., 'as long as it is eclipsed, it is dark' with the condition that it is eclipsed). The second interpretation takes it as asserting necessity as long as the description holds, i.e., throughout the entire duration of the description. Under the first interpretation, the relationship between the special conditional and the temporal (waqtiyya) is that the special conditional is more specific (akhas) absolutely, meaning it implies the temporal but not vice versa. The commentator explains (in note 2) that the necessity in the special conditional is relative to the essence at the time of the description, which is a specific time; thus the temporal necessity (which is also relative to a specific time) is implied. Conversely, if the description is not necessary for the essence in any time (like 'writer'), the special conditional can be true without the temporal. The temporal (waqtiyya) is then distinct from the two perpetual propositions (likely daima and maybe something else) and broader than the two general propositions (probably 'amma mutlaqa and 'amma mumkina) in some respects. The passage provides a meticulous comparison of modal propositions, essential for understanding the taxonomy of qadaya in Islamic logic.

Key Takeaway

The Definition of al-Muntashira (The Spread Proposition)
Author defines the sixth compound proposition, al-muntashira.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining characteristics of the muntashira proposition and distinguish it from other compound propositions.

Core Idea
The muntashira asserts necessity at an indefinite time, but never perpetually.

This lesson unpacks the definition of the sixth compound proposition, al-muntashira. You will learn its core conditions: necessity, unspecified time, and the crucial constraint of non-perpetuity. Understanding these will help you construct and evaluate temporal propositions in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the term being defined
The passage begins by naming the sixth compound proposition: al-muntashira. Identify what is being defined.
It is one of several compound propositions in the text.
2
Extract the defining features
The definition includes: (1) judgment of necessity of the predicate for the subject (or its negation), (2) at an unspecified time during the subject's existence, and (3) qualified by non-perpetuity (la da'im) according to the essence.
Non-perpetuity means it is not always true; there are times when the predicate does not hold.
3
Examine the affirmative example
The example 'Necessarily every human is breathing at some time, not always' shows an affirmative muntashira. Note that the necessity is attached to a temporal moment that is indefinite, and it is not perpetual (humans do not breathe before birth or after death).
4
Examine the negative example
The negative example 'Necessarily no human is breathing at some time, not always' states that there is an indefinite time when the predicate is necessarily absent, but not always.
This is a universal negative with temporal possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ ٦٠ - من القضايا المركبة: المنتشرة]
قال : السادسة المنتشرة، وهي التي حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه في وقت غير معين من أوقات وجود الموضوع مقيداً باللادوام بحسب الذات وهي إن كانت موجبة كقولنا: «بالضرورة كل إنسان متنفس في وقت ما لادائماً فتركيبها من موجبة منتشرة مطلقة وسالبة مطلقة عامة، وإن كانت سالبة كقولنا بالضرورة لاشيء من الإنسان بمتنفس في وقت ما لا دائماً» فتركيبها من سالبة منتشرة مطلقة وموجبة مطلقة عامة.
[60 - From the compound propositions: the spread] He said: The sixth is the spread (al-muntashira), which is that in which is judged the necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject or its negation from it in an unspecified time from the times of the subject's existence, qualified by non-perpetuity according to the essence. If it is affirmative, like our saying: 'Necessarily every human is breathing at some time, not always' – its composition is from an affirmative spread absolute and a negative general absolute. And if it is negative, like our saying: 'Necessarily no human is breathing at some time, not always' – its composition is from a negative spread absolute and an affirmative general absolute.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage introduces the sixth type of compound proposition (qadiyya murakkaba) called 'al-muntashira' (the spread/distributed). The definition states that it judges the necessity of the predicate's affirmation or negation for the subject at an unspecified time from the times of the subject's existence, with the condition that it is not perpetual (la da'im) according to the essence. In other words, the judgment is that the predicate holds for the subject necessarily at some indefinite time, but not always. The passage then gives two examples: affirmative: 'Necessarily every human is breathing at some time, not always' – meaning every human necessarily breathes at some point in their life, but not continuously. This proposition is composed of two parts: an affirmative spread absolute (muntashira mutlaqa) and a negative general absolute (saliba mutlaqa 'amma). The negative example: 'Necessarily no human is breathing at some time, not always' – i.e., humans are necessarily not breathing at some indefinite time (like in the womb or after death), but not always. Its composition is from a negative spread absolute and an affirmative general absolute. This definitional passage teaches the structure of a specific compound modal proposition, showing how it combines two simpler propositions to express a necessity that is temporally indefinite and non-perpetual.

اللادوام بحسب الذات
موجبة منتشرة مطلقة

Key Takeaway
A muntashira proposition asserts a necessary judgment at an indefinite time, but never perpetually.

The Composition of al-Muntashira
Author explains the composition of muntashira propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to decompose a muntashira proposition into its constituent spread absolute and general absolute propositions.

Core Idea
Every muntashira is a compound of a spread absolute and a general absolute.

This lesson focuses on the compositional structure of the muntashira. The passage shows that an affirmative muntashira combines an affirmative spread absolute with a negative general absolute, and a negative muntashira combines a negative spread absolute with an affirmative general absolute. You will learn how these components work together.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two component types
The passage states that the composition involves a 'spread absolute' (muntashira mutlaqa) and a 'general absolute' ('amma mutlaqa). Understand that these are simpler propositions that, when combined, yield the compound muntashira.
Spread absolute: a proposition that judges necessity at an indefinite time without the non-perpetuity condition. General absolute: a universal proposition without temporal specification.
2
Analyze the affirmative composition
For the affirmative example 'Necessarily every human is breathing at some time, not always', the composition is: (a) an affirmative spread absolute: 'Necessarily every human is breathing at some time' + (b) a negative general absolute: 'No human is breathing always' (since 'not always' negates perpetuity).
3
Analyze the negative composition
For the negative example 'Necessarily no human is breathing at some time, not always', the composition is: (a) a negative spread absolute: 'Necessarily no human is breathing at some time' + (b) an affirmative general absolute: 'Every human is breathing always'? Wait, careful: The passage says it composes from a negative spread absolute and an affirmative general absolute. The affirmative general absolute here would be something like 'Every human is breathing always'? But that seems contradictory. Actually the 'not always' in the negative example means that the negation is not perpetual, so there are times when the predicate holds. So the affirmative general absolute likely affirms that at some times the predicate holds? Let's read: 'Necessarily no human is breathing at some time, not always' means that there is a time when humans are necessarily not breathing, but this is not always (i.e., there are other times when they do breathe). The affirmative general absolute might be 'Every human is breathing at some time'? But that would be an affirmative spread, not general. The text says 'saliba muntashira mutlaqa' (negative spread absolute) and 'mujiba mutlaqa 'amma' (affirmative general absolute). The affirmative general absolute likely affirms the opposite of the non-perpetuity condition: 'Every human is breathing at some time'? But that is not general absolute; it's a spread. Let's trust the text: the negative composition is from a negative spread absolute and an affirmative general absolute. We'll explain as per text.
4
Apply to other examples
Try constructing a muntashira on a different subject, such as 'Necessarily every prophet receives revelation at some time, not always.' Identify its components.

Defining al-Waqtiyya (Time-Specific Proposition)
Defines al-waqtiyya and begins its modal comparison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-waqtiyya as a time-specific necessity proposition and distinguish it from perpetual necessity.

Core Idea
Al-waqtiyya ties necessity to a specific moment of the subject's existence.

In this passage, al-Qutb al-Tahtani defines the time-specific proposition (al-waqtiyya). He explains that necessity is realized at some times of the subject's essence, not perpetually. Understanding this definition is crucial for correctly applying modal propositions in Islamic logic, especially in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic passage. It is 'الوقتية' (al-waqtiyya).
2
Extract the core description
The passage says 'تحققت الضرورة في بعض أوقات الذات' – necessity is realized in some times of the essence. This is the defining feature: the necessity is temporally bound to the subject's existence.
3
Note what is excluded
The phrase 'و من غير عكس' indicates that this is not reversible: the necessity does not hold at all times, nor does it apply to the opposite (i.e., the negation is not necessary at other times).
This distinguishes it from perpetual propositions where necessity holds at all times.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوصف بعض أوقات الذات تحققت الضرورة في بعض أوقات الذات و من غير عكس.
والوقتية مباينة للدائمتين وأعم من العامتين من وجه لصدقها في مادة المشروطة الخاصة وصدقهما بدونها في مادة الضرورة بالعكس حيث
لادوام بحسب الوصف.
وأخص من المطلقة العامة والممكنة العامة.
The description: in some times of the essence, necessity is realized in some times of the essence, and not conversely. And the time-specific is opposite to the two perpetuals and more general than the two general in aspect, because it is true in the matter of the conditional-proper and both are true without it in the matter of necessity conversely where there is no perpetuity according to the description. And it is more specific than the general absolute and the general possible.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage defines the time-specific proposition and contrasts it with other modalities. It begins by describing the time-specific as involving necessity in some times of the essence without the reverse. Then it states that the time-specific (waqtiyya) is opposite to the two perpetuals (daimatayn) and more general than the two general propositions (ammatayn) in a certain respect. The explanation: the time-specific is true in the matter of the conditional-proper (mashruta khassa), while the two perpetuals are true without it in the matter of necessity conversely, where there is no perpetuity according to description. Finally, it states that the time-specific is more specific than the general absolute (mutlaqa amma) and the general possible (mumkina amma). This establishes the logical relationships between these modal propositions used in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
الوقتية
المشروطة الخاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Al-waqtiyya is a time-specific necessity: it applies only at certain times of the subject's existence.

Logical Relations of al-Waqtiyya
Defines al-waqtiyya and compares it to other modal propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the logical relationships between al-waqtiyya and the other modal propositions mentioned in the passage.

Core Idea
Al-waqtiyya stands opposite to perpetuals and lies between the general and specific modalities.

After defining al-waqtiyya, the passage situates it within the modal system by stating its relations to the two perpetuals (daimatayn), the two generals (ammatayn), the conditional-proper (mashruta khassa), the general absolute (mutlaqa amma), and the general possible (mumkina amma). Mapping these relations is essential for correctly using modal propositions in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
List the relevant terms
Identify all modal types mentioned: al-waqtiyya, al-daimatayn, al-ammatayn, al-mashruta al-khassa, al-mutlaqa al-amma, al-mumkina al-amma.
2
Find the relation statements
Locate the claims: waqtiyya is 'mubayina' (opposite) to daimatayn, 'a'am' (more general) than ammatayn, and 'akhass' (more specific) than mutlaqa amma and mumkina amma.
3
Understand the justification for each relation
For the generality claim, the passage explains: waqtiyya is true in the matter of mashruta khassa (conditional-proper), while the two perpetuals are true without it in the matter of necessity conversely, where there is no perpetuity according to description. This shows a partial overlap.
4
Visualize the hierarchy
Arrange the modalities: perpetuals are opposite to waqtiyya; waqtiyya is more general than the two generals but more specific than the general absolute and possible. This creates a layered relationship.

The Two Types of al-Mashruta al-Khassa
Distinguishes two types of conditional-proper

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of al-mashruta al-khassa: the one with the mentioned meaning and the one with the condition of description.

Core Idea
The conditional-proper proposition splits into two varieties depending on whether necessity attaches to the essence alone or to the essence together with a description.

In his commentary, al-Sharif al-Jurjani reveals that the term 'al-mashruta al-khassa' is not univocal. It covers two distinct logical forms. Recognizing this distinction is essential for correctly applying modal logic in Islamic arguments, particularly when analyzing conditional statements in theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Locate in the Arabic the phrases 'المشروطة الخاصة بالمعنى المذكور' and 'المشروطة الخاصة بشرط الوصف'. These are the two types.
The first is defined earlier in the text; the second is introduced here.
2
Understand the difference
The first type involves necessity relative to the subject's essence (ذات الموضوع) alone. The second type involves necessity relative to the essence taken together with a description (الذات مأخوذاً مع الوصف).
This is a crucial nuance in modal logic.
3
See the logical consequence
Each type has a different relationship with the time-specific proposition (الوقتية). The first is less general than time-specific, while the second can be true without time-specific being true.
This shows they are not interchangeable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشروطة الخاصة بالمعنى المذكور صدقت الوقتية، وتصدق الوقتية في المثال المذكور بدون المشروطة الخاصة فتكون الوقتية أعم منها مطلقاً.
وأما المشروطة الخاصة بشرط الوصف فيمكن صدقها بدون الوقتية كما في مثال الكتابة وتحرك الأصابع، فإنّ المحمول هناك ليس ضروري النسبة إلى ذات الموضوع في زمان الوصف، بل هو ضروري النسبة بالقياس إلى الذات مأخوذاً مع الوصف - كما تقرر - ومعنى الوقتية الضرورة في وقت معين بالقياس إلى الذات وحده فلا تصدق هناك (شريف).
The conditional-proper in the mentioned meaning is true of the time-specific, and the time-specific is true in the mentioned example without the conditional-proper, so the time-specific is absolutely more general than it. As for the conditional-proper with the condition of description, it can be true without the time-specific, as in the example of writing and moving the fingers, for the predicate there is not a necessary relation to the essence of the subject in the time of description, but it is a necessary relation relative to the essence taken together with the description - as established - and the meaning of time-specific is necessity at a specific time relative to the essence alone, so it is not true there. (al-Sharif)
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage clarifies the logical relationship between the time-specific (waqtiyya) and two types of conditional-proper (mashruta khassa). The first part states that under the previously mentioned meaning (which likely refers to the conditional-proper as defined earlier), the time-specific holds and is actually true in the example given, but the conditional-proper is not necessary for the time-specific to be true. Hence the time-specific is absolutely more general than that type of conditional-proper. The second part discusses the conditional-proper with the condition of description (bi-shart al-wasf). It can be true without the time-specific, as illustrated by the example of writing and moving fingers. In that example, the predicate (writing) is not necessary for the essence at the time of the description (the act of writing), but it is necessary relative to the essence when taken together with the description (i.e., the human is a writer while writing). The time-specific, which requires necessity at a specific time relative to the essence alone, does not hold there because the necessity disappears when the description is removed. This demonstrates that the conditional-proper with description condition is more specific in some cases.

Key Terms
المشروطة الخاصة
بشرط الوصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The first type has necessity relative to the essence alone, the second relative to essence with a description.
C) The first type is true in all cases, the second is false.
D) There is no difference; they are synonyms.

Key Takeaway
Al-mashruta al-khassa has two forms: one with necessity attached to the essence alone, and one with necessity attached to the essence plus a description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'If a person writes, then his fingers move.' Determine whether this is an example of al-mashruta al-khassa with the condition of description. Explain why the time-specific version would be false here, using the reasoning from the passage.

Generality Relations: Time-Specific vs. Conditional-Proper
Compares generality of time-specific and conditional-proper types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the logical relationship (general-specific) between time-specific (waqtiyya) and each type of conditional-proper (mashruta khassa).

Core Idea
The time-specific proposition is absolutely more general than one type of conditional-proper, but the other type can be true without the time-specific.

This passage not only distinguishes two types of conditional-proper but also clarifies their logical relations to the time-specific proposition. Understanding these relations is key to correctly applying modal propositions in Islamic logical analysis, especially when evaluating the strength of arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two types
Remember from the previous lesson that there are two types of mashruta khassa: one with the 'mentioned meaning' and one with 'condition of description'.
2
Analyze the first type
The passage says: 'The conditional-proper in the mentioned meaning is true of the time-specific, and the time-specific is true ... without the conditional-proper.' This means the time-specific is more general (a'am mutlaqan) than this type.
The first type is a species of time-specific.
3
Analyze the second type
For the second type (bi-shart al-wasf), the passage gives the example of writing and moving fingers. Here the conditional-proper holds (writing is necessary when the person is writing), but the time-specific does not, because the necessity is not relative to the essence alone at a specific time.
Thus the second type is not a species of time-specific; it has a different logical form.
4
Synthesize the overall relationship
The first type is subordinate to time-specific; the second type can be independent. Therefore, one cannot treat all conditional-proper propositions as equivalent to time-specific.
This distinction prevents logical errors in modal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشروطة الخاصة بالمعنى المذكور صدقت الوقتية، وتصدق الوقتية في المثال المذكور بدون المشروطة الخاصة فتكون الوقتية أعم منها مطلقاً.
وأما المشروطة الخاصة بشرط الوصف فيمكن صدقها بدون الوقتية كما في مثال الكتابة وتحرك الأصابع، فإنّ المحمول هناك ليس ضروري النسبة إلى ذات الموضوع في زمان الوصف، بل هو ضروري النسبة بالقياس إلى الذات مأخوذاً مع الوصف - كما تقرر - ومعنى الوقتية الضرورة في وقت معين بالقياس إلى الذات وحده فلا تصدق هناك (شريف).

As for the conditional-proper with the condition of description, it can be true without the time-specific, as in the example of writing and moving the fingers, for the predicate there is not a necessary relation to the essence of the subject in the time of description, but it is a necessary relation relative to the essence taken together with the description - as established - and the meaning of time-specific is necessity at a specific time relative to the essence alone, so it is not true there.
